{"uri": "8119125203", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "12:24:11", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T12:24:11Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T12:23:50Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9333333373069763, "url": "https://www.irishnews.com/news/uk/five-babies-in-england-die-as-whooping-cough-cases-soar-RQMYSXOLIZLJDAE3QRTDBR43QA/", "title": "Five babies die as whooping cough cases soar", "body": "UK Health Security Agency data shows there were 2,793 cases reported to the end of March, compared to 858 for the whole of 2023.\n\nFive babies in England have died after being diagnosed with whooping cough, health officials have said amid a rapid rise in cases.\n\nMore than 2,700 whooping cough cases have been reported across England so far in 2024 - more than three times the number recorded in the whole of last year.\n\nNew UK Health Security Agency (UKHSA) figures show there were 2,793 cases reported to the end of March.\n\nThat compares to 858 cases for the whole of 2023.\n\nThe UKHSA said between January and the end of March, there have been five infant deaths.\n\nUKHSA consultant epidemiologist Dr Gayatri Amirthalingam said: \"Whooping cough can affect people of all ages, but for very young babies it can be extremely serious.\n\n\"Our thoughts and condolences are with those families who have so tragically lost their baby.\"\n\nIn March alone, some 1,319 cases were reported, according to the provisional data.\n\nThe bacterial infection, also known as pertussis, affects the lungs and breathing tubes.\n\nWhooping cough can be called the \"100-day cough\" because of how long it can take to recover from it, and it spreads very easily.\n\nThere were 556 cases recorded in January and 918 in February.\n\nPregnant women are being urged to take up the offer of the whooping cough vaccine so they can pass on protection to their babies, which should last until they are old enough to get vaccinated themselves.\n\nBetween January and the end of March, 108 babies under the age of three months were diagnosed with whooping cough. Some 51% of cases during this period were among those aged 15 and older.\n\nHealth officials describe whooping cough as a \"cyclical disease\", which means it peaks every few years. With whooping cough this is every three to five years.\n\nThe last big increase was seen in 2016, but cases dipped to very low numbers during the coronavirus pandemic which means the current peak is \"overdue\", the UKHSA said.\n\nIt added the impact of the pandemic means there is \"reduced immunity in the population\".\n\nUKHSA also said vaccine uptake has fallen in recent years - both the jabs for pregnant women and children.\n\nDr Amirthalingam added: \"Vaccination remains the best defence against whooping cough and it is vital that pregnant women and young infants receive their vaccines at the right time.\"\n\nThe NHS recommends all pregnant women are vaccinated against whooping cough between 16 and 32 weeks.\n\nImmunity from the jab passes through the placenta to protect newborn babies in their first weeks of life.\n\nWhen a baby is eight weeks old they are offered the six-in-one vaccine, which includes immunisation against whooping cough.\n\nThe second dose of the vaccine is offered at 12 weeks and the third is offered at 16 weeks.\n\nWhen children are three years and four months they will be offered the four-in-one pre-school booster, which protects against pertussis.\n\nProfessor Sir Stephen Powis, national medical director for the NHS in England, said: \"With cases of whooping cough continuing to rise sharply across the country, and today's figures sadly showing five infant deaths, it is vital that families come forward to get the protection they need.\n\n\"If you are pregnant and have not been vaccinated yet, or your child is not up-to-date with whooping cough or other routine vaccinations, please contact your GP as soon as possible, and if you or your child show symptoms ask for an urgent GP appointment or get help from NHS 111.\"\n\nUKHSA said the first signs of whooping cough are similar to a cold, such as a runny nose and sore throat, but after about a week, the infection can develop into coughing bouts that last for a few minutes and are typically worse at night.\n\nYoung babies may also make a distinctive \"whoop\" or have difficulty breathing after a bout of coughing, though not all babies make this noise which means whooping cough can be hard to recognise.\n\nCommenting on the figures, Dr Michael Head, senior research fellow in global health at the University of Southampton, said: \"This new data alas illustrates just how serious whooping cough is - it can and does kill babies.\n\n\"Whooping cough can have a very long infectious period of around two to three weeks, i.e the period of time when an infected individual is able to transmit the bacteria onto others. Thus, there may be plenty of opportunities for people to mix, transmission to occur, and for there to be an infection of a vulnerable individual, such as a baby.\n\n\"A high coverage of vaccination is vital in reducing transmission within populations. Vaccine uptake in young children and for expectant mothers during pregnancy is so important. The vaccine is safe, and extremely effective.\"\n\nProfessor Andrew Preston, from the Milner Centre for Evolution at the University of Bath, added: \"It is extremely sad news to hear of the deaths of five babies, especially as whooping cough is a vaccine preventable disease.\n\n\"While no vaccine is perfect, pertussis vaccination of mums during pregnancy and of infants protect the babies and infants who are the most vulnerable to this serious, and sometimes fatal, disease.\"", "source": {"uri": "irishnews.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "The Irish News"}, "authors": [], "concepts": That compares to 858 cases for the whole of 2023.\n\nREAD MORE: 'Beautiful' mum, 36, dies suddenly as family pay tribute\n\nREAD MORE: Best friends paid the 'ultimate price' after travelling to Cheshire Oaks\n\nThe UKHSA said between January and the end of March, there have been five infant deaths.\n\nUKHSA consultant epidemiologist Dr Gayatri Amirthalingam said: \"Whooping cough can affect people of all ages, but for very young babies it can be extremely serious. Our thoughts and condolences are with those families who have so tragically lost their baby.\"\n\nIn March alone, some 1,319 cases were reported, according to the provisional data.\n\nThe bacterial infection, also known as pertussis, affects the lungs and breathing tubes. Whooping cough can be called the \"100-day cough\" because of how long it can take to recover from it, and it spreads very easily.\n\nThere were 556 cases recorded in January and 918 in February.\n\nPregnant women are being urged to take up the offer of the whooping cough vaccine so they can pass on protection to their babies, which should last until they are old enough to get vaccinated themselves.\n\nBetween January and the end of March, 108 babies under the age of three months were diagnosed with whooping cough. Some 51% of cases during this period were among those aged 15 and older.\n\nHealth officials describe whooping cough as a \"cyclical disease\", which means it peaks every few years. With whooping cough this is every three to five years.\n\nThe last big increase was seen in 2016, but cases dipped to very low numbers during the coronavirus pandemic which means the current peak is \"overdue\", the UKHSA said.\n\nIt added the impact of the pandemic means there is \"reduced immunity in the population\". UKHSA also said vaccine uptake has fallen in recent years both the jabs for pregnant women and children.\n\nDr Amirthalingam added: \"Vaccination remains the best defence against whooping cough and it is vital that pregnant women and young infants receive their vaccines at the right time.\"\n\nThe NHS recommends all pregnant women are vaccinated against whooping cough between 16 and 32 weeks. Immunity from the jab passes through the placenta to protect newborn babies in their first weeks of life.\n\nWhen a baby is eight weeks old they are offered the six-in-one vaccine, which includes immunisation against whooping cough.\n\nThe second dose of the vaccine is offered at 12 weeks and the third is offered at 16 weeks.\n\nWhen children are three years and four months they will be offered the four-in-one pre-school booster, which protects against pertussis.\n\nProfessor Sir Stephen Powis, national medical director for the NHS in England, said: \"With cases of whooping cough continuing to rise sharply across the country, and today's figures sadly showing five infant deaths, it is vital that families come forward to get the protection they need.\n\n\"If you are pregnant and have not been vaccinated yet, or your child is not up-to-date with whooping cough or other routine vaccinations, please contact your GP as soon as possible, and if you or your child show symptoms ask for an urgent GP appointment or get help from NHS 111.\"\n\nUKHSA said the first signs of whooping cough are similar to a cold, such as a runny nose and sore throat, but after about a week, the infection can develop into coughing bouts that last for a few minutes and are typically worse at night.\n\nYoung babies may also make a distinctive \"whoop\" or have difficulty breathing after a bout of coughing, though not all babies make this noise which means whooping cough can be hard to recognise.\n\nCommenting on the figures, Dr Michael Head, senior research fellow in global health at the University of Southampton, said: \"This new data alas illustrates just how serious whooping cough is it can and does kill babies.\n\n\"Whooping cough can have a very long infectious period of around two to three weeks, i.e the period of time when an infected individual is able to transmit the bacteria onto others. Thus, there may be plenty of opportunities for people to mix, transmission to occur, and for there to be an infection of a vulnerable individual, such as a baby.\n\n\"A high coverage of vaccination is vital in reducing transmission within populations. Vaccine uptake in young children and for expectant mothers during pregnancy is so important. {"uri": "2024-05-346267922", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-06", "time": "09:27:03", "dateTime": "2024-05-06T09:27:03Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-06T09:13:50Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9137254953384399, "url": "https://eu.greenvilleonline.com/story/news/local/greenville/2024/05/06/heres-how-to-prevent-the-spread-of-whooping-cough-in-south-carolina/73553520007/", "title": "Lingering cough that won't go away? If you live in SC, get checked for whooping cough", "body": "Whooping cough cases have been reported in South Carolina with a rise from last year's numbers.\n\nAccording to the South Carolina Department of Health and Environmental Control (DHEC), SC has seen a total of 52 whooping cough cases in total in 2024. In the Upstate, 47 cases were reported, followed by four in the Lowcountry and one in the Midlands.\n\nLast year, the state only reported a total of six cases.\n\nDr. Martha Buchanan, interim director of the Bureau of Communicable Disease Prevention and Control at DHEC, does not consider the numbers unusual.\n\n\"If you look at our data, I mention the rise in cases. But if we look back from 2017 to about 2023 to see complete data from those years' cases, some years we have 200 cases in South Carolina. Some years we only have about 30 cases. So it's natural for us to see case rates for all diseases to go up and down. They're kind of cyclical,\" Buchanan said.\n\nWhat is whooping cough?\n\nWhooping cough, also known as pertussis, is a highly contagious bacterial infection that causes symptoms like coughing in the respiratory tract. The cough will oftentimes be accompanied by a \"whoop\" sound at the end of a coughing attack. In severe cases, shortness of breath, fever, fatigue, and loss of appetite may occur, according to Buchanan. If you have a lingering cough that has not gone away as expected, scheduling an appointment with your primary health physician to get tested for pertussis is the best course of action.\n\nHow is the infection transmitted?\n\nLike most respiratory illnesses, whooping cough is spread when an infected individual coughs or sneezes. The respiratory droplets are then breathed in by nearby individuals.\n\nHow is the infection prevented?\n\nVaccination is the best way to protect yourself and others from coming down with pertussis. Effective and safe vaccinations against infection include the following:\n\n\u220e Diphtheria, tetanus and pertussis (DTaP) vaccines: This vaccine is intended for babies and children under seven years of age.\n\n\u220e Tetanus, diphtheria, and pertussis (Tdap) vaccines: This vaccine is intended for older children and adults.\n\nIf you do get pertussis, DHEC strongly recommends staying home until you recover from the sickness, covering your cough, and washing your hands.\n\n\"It's a preventable disease and we really encourage everybody to make sure they stay up to date on their vaccines,\" said Buchanan.\n\nWho is susceptible to whooping cough?\n\nPeople who are not vaccinated with the DTaP or Tdap vaccines are more likely to contract pertussis. Preteens, teenagers, and the elderly are also at higher risk of infection. Infants, in particular, should be kept away from those who have pertussis because they cannot receive the vaccination.\n\n\"Pertussis can be dangerous for very young infants because their airways are so small and because of inflammation of the airways. It can really cause a lot of babies to end up in the hospital if they get pertussis.\" Buchanan said.", "source": {"uri": "greenvilleonline.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "The Greenville News"}, It comes as the number of cases of the bacterial infection in England have soared in recent months.\n\nMore than 2,700 whooping cough cases have been reported so far in 2024 - more than three times the number recorded in the whole of last year. ew UK Health Security Agency (UKHSA) figures show there were 2,793 cases reported to the end of March.\n\nThat is a huge increase compared to 858 cases for the whole of 2023. And the UKHSA said between January and the end of March, there have been five infant deaths.\n\nREAD MORE: Win a \u00a3200 Wickes voucher to celebrate the launch of Kimberley Walsh's new paint\n\nGet breaking news on BirminghamLive WhatsApp\n\nUKHSA consultant epidemiologist Dr Gayatri Amirthalingam said: \"Whooping cough can affect people of all ages, but for very young babies it can be extremely serious. Our thoughts and condolences are with those families who have so tragically lost their baby.\"\n\nIn March alone, 1,319 cases were reported, according to the provisional data. The condition is also known as pertussis and affects the lungs and breathing tubes. It can be called the \"100-day cough\" because of how long it can take to recover from it, and it spreads very easily.\n\nThere were 556 cases recorded in January and 918 in February. Pregnant women are being urged to take up the offer of the whooping cough vaccine so they can pass on protection to their babies, which should last until they are old enough to get vaccinated themselves.\n\nBetween January and the end of March, 108 babies under the age of three months were diagnosed with whooping cough. Some 51 per cent of cases during this period were among those aged 15 and older.\n\nHealth officials describe whooping cough as a \"cyclical disease\", which means it peaks every few years. With whooping cough this is every three to five years.\n\nThe last big increase was seen in 2016, but cases dipped to very low numbers during the coronavirus pandemic which means the current peak is \"overdue\", the UKHSA said. It added the impact of the pandemic means there is \"reduced immunity in the population\".\n\nUKHSA also said vaccine uptake has fallen in recent years - both the jabs for pregnant women and children.\n\nDr Amirthalingam said: \"Vaccination remains the best defence against whooping cough and it is vital that pregnant women and young infants receive their vaccines at the right time.\"\n\nThe NHS recommends all pregnant women are vaccinated against whooping cough between 16 and 32 weeks of their pregnancy. Immunity from the jab passes through the placenta to protect newborn babies in their first weeks of life.\n\nWhen a baby is eight weeks old they are offered the six-in-one vaccine, which includes immunisation against whooping cough. The second dose of the vaccine is offered at 12 weeks and the third is offered at 16 weeks.\n\nWhen children are three years and four months they will be offered the four-in-one pre-school booster, which protects against pertussis.\n\nProfessor Sir Stephen Powis, national medical director for the NHS in England, said: \"With cases of whooping cough continuing to rise sharply across the country, and today's figures sadly showing five infant deaths, it is vital that families come forward to get the protection they need.\n\n\"If you are pregnant and have not been vaccinated yet, or your child is not up-to-date with whooping cough or other routine vaccinations, please contact your GP as soon as possible, and if you or your child show symptoms ask for an urgent GP appointment or get help from NHS 111.\"\n\nUKHSA said the first signs of whooping cough are similar to a cold, such as a runny nose and sore throat. After about a week, the infection can develop into coughing bouts that last for a few minutes and are typically worse at night.\n\nYoung babies may also make a distinctive \"whoop\" or have difficulty breathing after a bout of coughing, though not all babies make this noise which means whooping cough can be hard to recognise.\n\nDr Michael Head, senior research fellow in global health at the University of Southampton, said: \"This new data alas illustrates just how serious whooping cough is - it can and does kill babies. Whooping cough can have a very long infectious period of around two to three weeks, i.e the period of time when an infected individual is able to transmit the bacteria onto others.\n\n\"Thus, there may be plenty of opportunities for people to mix, transmission to occur, and for there to be an infection of a vulnerable individual, such as a baby. A high coverage of vaccination is vital in reducing transmission within populations. Vaccine uptake in young children and for expectant mothers during pregnancy is so important. The vaccine is safe, and extremely effective.\"\n\nProfessor Andrew Preston, from the Milner Centre for Evolution at the University of Bath, added: \"It is extremely sad news to hear of the deaths of five babies, especially as whooping cough is a vaccine preventable disease.\n\n\"While no vaccine is perfect, pertussis vaccination of mums during pregnancy and of infants protect the babies and Here the PA news agency answers commonly-asked questions on the condition.\n\nWhat is whooping cough?\n\nThe bacterial infection, also known as pertussis, affects the lungs and breathing tubes. Whooping cough is sometimes called the \"100-day cough\" because of how long it can take to recover from it, and it spreads very easily.\n\nWhat are the symptoms of whooping cough?\n\nThe first signs of whooping cough are similar to a cold and can include a runny nose and sore throat, but after about a week, the infection can develop into coughing bouts that last for a few minutes and are typically worse at night. Young babies may also make a distinctive \"whoop\" or have difficulty breathing after a bout of coughing, though not all babies make this noise.\n\nWho is most affected?\n\nThe majority of cases (51%) have been diagnosed among people aged 15 and over but the condition is most serious in babies. Between January and the end of March, 108 babies under the age of three months were diagnosed with whooping cough.\n\nHow many cases have there been?\n\nThere were 2,793 cases reported between January and the end of March, according to UK Health Security Agency (UKHSA) figures. In March alone, some 1,319 cases were reported. There were 556 cases recorded in January and 918 in February.\n\nHow does this compare to previous years?\n\nThere were 858 cases for the whole of 2023.\n\nWhy are cases so high?\n\nHealth officials at the UKHSA describe whooping cough as a \"cyclical disease\", which means it peaks every few years. With whooping cough this peak usually occurs every three to five years. The last big increase was seen in 2016.\n\nBut cases dipped to very low numbers during the coronavirus pandemic - likely due to a lack of socialising and social distancing measures - which means the current peak is \"overdue\", the UKHSA said. It added the impact of the pandemic means there is \"reduced immunity in the population\". Vaccination rates have also decreased in recent years.\n\nWhat can people do to protect themselves?\n\nPregnant women are being urged to take up the offer of the whooping cough vaccine so they can pass on protection to their babies, which should last until they are old enough to get vaccinated themselves. Parents are also being advised to take up the offer of vaccination for children when called forward.\n\nWhen can people get vaccinated?\n\nThe NHS recommends all pregnant women are vaccinated against whooping cough between 16 and 32 weeks. When a baby is eight weeks old they are offered the six-in-one vaccine, which includes immunisation against whooping cough.\n\nThe second dose of the vaccine is offered at 12 weeks and the third is offered at 16 weeks. {"uri": "8119093923", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "12:04:09", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T12:04:09Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T12:03:50Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9058823585510254, "url": "https://www.chroniclelive.co.uk/news/health/five-babies-die-after-whooping-29137626", "title": "Five babies die after whooping cough diagnosis as cases in England soar", "body": "Five babies have died in England after being diagnosed with whooping cough amid a sharp rise in cases., health officials have confirmed.\n\nMore than 2,700 whooping cough cases have been reported across England so far in 2024 - more than three times the number recorded in the whole of last year. New figures from the UK Health Security Agency (UKHSA) show there were 2,793 cases reported to the end of March, compared to 858 cases for the whole of 2023.\n\nThe UKHSA said between January and the end of March, there have been five infant deaths from the bacterial infection, also known as pertussis, the PA news agency reports. Whooping cough, which affects the lungs and breathing tubes, is highly contagious and is sometimes referred to as the \"100-day cough\" because of how long it can take to recover from its symptoms.\n\nREAD MORE: Four early signs your child might have autism including difficulties with communication\n\nKeep up to date with all the latest breaking news and top stories from the North East with our free newsletter\n\nUKHSA consultant epidemiologist Dr Gayatri Amirthalingam said of the figures: \"Whooping cough can affect people of all ages, but for very young babies it can be extremely serious. Our thoughts and condolences are with those families who have so tragically lost their baby.\"\n\nHealth officials describe whooping cough as a \"cyclical disease\", which means it peaks every three to five years. The last big increase was seen in 2016, but cases dipped to very low numbers during the coronavirus pandemic - which means the current peak is \"overdue\", the UKHSA said.\n\nHealth officials added that the impact of the pandemic means there is \"reduced immunity in the population\". In March alone, some 1,319 cases were reported, according to the provisional data, while there were 556 cases recorded in January and 918 in February.\n\nSymptoms of whooping cough\n\nAccording to the NHS, the first signs of whooping cough are similar to a cold, such as a runny nose and sore throat, although a high temperature is uncommon. The main symptoms as listed by the health service, which will appear after around a week and can last for several weeks or months, include:\n\nCoughing bouts that last for a few minutes and are worse at night A \"whoop\" sound - a gasp for breath between coughs (young babies and some adults may not \"whoop\") Difficulty breathing after a coughing bout and may turn blue or grey (young infants) Bringing up a thick mucus, which can make you vomit Becoming very red in the face (more common in adults)\n\nIf you or your child has symptoms of whooping cough, it's important to stay off school, work or nursery until 48 hours after starting antibiotics, or three weeks after your symptoms started if you have not had antibiotics.\n\nYou should ask for an urgent GP appointment or seek help from NHS 111 if your baby is under six months old and has symptoms of whooping cough, or if you or your child have a very bad cough that is getting worse. You should also seek medical advice if you have been in contact with someone who has whooping cough and you're pregnant, or you have a weakened immune system.\n\nWhooping cough can also lead to serious complications, particularly in babies under six months old, including dehydration, breathing difficulties, pneumonia and seizures. You should call 999 or go to A&E if you or your child's lips, tongue, face or skin suddenly turn blue or grey, if they are finding it hard to breathe properly, or if they have chest pain that is worse when breathing or coughing, as this could be a sign of pneumonia.\n\nTreatment for whooping cough\n\nTreatment for whooping cough depends on your age and how long you have had the infection. Hospital treatment is usually needed if you have severe whooping cough, or your baby is under six months old and has whooping cough, according to the NHS website.\n\nIf whooping cough is diagnosed within three weeks, you will be given antibiotics to help stop it spreading to others - antibiotics may not reduce your symptoms, but it's important to take the whole course in order to prevent the infection from coming back. If you have had whooping cough for more than three weeks, you're no longer contagious and do not need antibiotics.\n\nAnyone suffering from whooping cough is advised to get plenty of rest and drink lots of fluids, and paracetamol or ibuprofen can help to relieve discomfort. However, the NHS urges against cough medicines, as they are not suitable for young children and will not help with this type of cough.\n\nWho can get the whooping cough vaccine?\n\nPregnant women are being urged to take up the offer of the whooping cough vaccine so they can pass on protection to their babies, which should last until they are old enough to get vaccinated themselves. The NHS recommends all pregnant women are vaccinated against whooping cough between 16 and 32 weeks.\n\nWhen a baby is eight weeks old they are offered the six-in-one vaccine, which includes immunisation against whooping cough. The second dose of the vaccine is offered at 12 weeks and the third is offered at 16 weeks, and when children are three years and four months they will be offered the four-in-one pre-school booster, which protects against pertussis among other infections.\n\nProfessor Sir Stephen Powis, national medical director for the NHS in England, said: \"With cases of whooping cough continuing to rise sharply across the country, and today's figures sadly showing five infant deaths, it is vital that families come forward to get the protection they need.\n\n\"If you are pregnant and have not been vaccinated yet, or your child is not up-to-date with whooping cough or other routine vaccinations, please contact your GP as soon as possible, and if you or your child show symptoms ask for an urgent GP appointment or get help from NHS 111.\"", "source": {"uri": "chroniclelive.co.uk", "dataType": "news", "title": "Chronicle Live"}, "authors": [{"uri": "ella_pickover@chroniclelive.co.uk", "name": If you live in SC, get checked for whooping cough", "body": "Whooping cough cases have been reported in South Carolina with a rise from last year's numbers.\n\nAccording to the South Carolina Department of Health and Environmental Control (DHEC), SC has seen a total of 52 whooping cough cases in total in 2024. In the Upstate, 47 cases were reported, followed by four in the Lowcountry and one in the Midlands.\n\nLast year, the state only reported a total of six cases.\n\nDr. Martha Buchanan, interim director of the Bureau of Communicable Disease Prevention and Control at DHEC, does not consider the numbers unusual.\n\n\"If you look at our data, I mention the rise in cases. But if we look back from 2017 to about 2023 to see complete data from those years' cases, some years we have 200 cases in South Carolina. Some years we only have about 30 cases. So it's natural for us to see case rates for all diseases to go up and down. They're kind of cyclical,\" Buchanan said.\n\nWhooping cough, also known as pertussis, is a highly contagious bacterial infection that causes symptoms like coughing in the respiratory tract. The cough will oftentimes be accompanied by a \"whoop\" sound at the end of a coughing attack. In severe cases, shortness of breath, fever, fatigue, and loss of appetite may occur, according to Buchanan. If you have a lingering cough that has not gone away as expected, scheduling an appointment with your primary health physician to get tested for pertussis is the best course of action.\n\nLike most respiratory illnesses, whooping cough is spread when an infected individual coughs or sneezes. The respiratory droplets are then breathed in by nearby individuals.\n\nVaccination is the best way to protect yourself and others from coming down with pertussis. Effective and safe vaccinations against infection include the following:\n\n\u220e Diphtheria, tetanus and pertussis (DTaP) vaccines: This vaccine is intended for babies and children under seven years of age.\n\n\u220e Tetanus, diphtheria, and pertussis (Tdap) vaccines: This vaccine is intended for older children and adults.\n\nIf you do get pertussis, DHEC strongly recommends staying home until you recover from the sickness, covering your cough, and washing your hands.\n\n\"It's a preventable disease and we really encourage everybody to make sure they stay up to date on their vaccines,\" said Buchanan.\n\nPeople who are not vaccinated with the DTaP or Tdap vaccines are more likely to contract pertussis. Preteens, teenagers, and the elderly are also at higher risk of infection. Infants, in particular, should be kept away from those who have pertussis because they cannot receive the vaccination.\n\n\"Pertussis can be dangerous for very young infants because their airways are so small and because of inflammation of the airways. It can really cause a lot of babies to end up in the hospital if they get pertussis.\" Buchanan said.\n\nNina Tran covers trending topics. New data published on Thursday, May 9 by the UK Health Security Agency (UKHSA) shows cases of whooping cough continue to increase with 1,319 cases confirmed in March.\n\nThis follows 556 cases in January and 918 in February, bringing the total number of cases in 2024 to 2,793. Sadly, in the first quarter of 2024 from January to March, there have been five infant deaths. Young infants are at highest risk of severe complications and death from whooping cough, according to the UKHSA.\n\nWhile most cases were in those aged 15 years or older who usually get a mild illness, the rates of whooping cough remain highest in babies under three months of age.\n\nDr Vanessa MacGregor, Consultant in Communicable Disease Control at UKHSA East Midlands, said: \"Vaccination remains the best defence against whooping cough and it is vital that pregnant women and young infants receive their vaccines at the right time.\n\n\"Pregnant women are offered a whooping cough vaccine in every pregnancy, ideally between 20 and 32 weeks. This passes protection to their baby in the womb so that they are protected from birth in the first months of their life when they are most vulnerable and before they can receive their own vaccines.\n\n\"All babies are given three doses of the six in one jab at eight, 12 and 16 weeks of age to protect against whooping cough and other serious diseases such as diphtheria and polio with a pre-school booster offered at 3 years 4 months.\n\n\"Whooping cough can affect people of all ages but for very young babies it can be extremely serious. Our thoughts and condolences are with those families who have so tragically lost their baby.\"\n\nWhooping cough cases have been rising across England and other countries since December 2023. It is a cyclical disease that peaks every three to five years.\n\nThe last cyclical increase occurred in 2016. However, in common with other diseases, cases fell to very low numbers during the pandemic due to restrictions and public behaviours. A peak year is therefore overdue. The impact of the pandemic also means there is reduced immunity in the population.\n\nUptake of vaccinations that protect against whooping cough have fallen in recent years across the country - in both the programme for pregnant women and the infant programme. Timely vaccination in pregnancy and in infancy are both important to protect vulnerable young babies from serious disease.\n\nNHS National Medical Director, Professor Sir Stephen Powis, said: \"With cases of whooping cough continuing to rise sharply across the country, and today's figures sadly showing five infant deaths, it is vital that families come forward to get the protection they need.\n\n\"If you are pregnant and have not been vaccinated yet, or your child is not up-to-date with whooping cough or other routine vaccinations, please contact your GP as soon as possible, and if you or your child show symptoms ask for an urgent GP appointment or get help from NHS 111.\n\n\"Whooping cough, clinically known as pertussis, is a bacterial infection which affects the lungs. The first signs of infection are similar to a cold, such as a runny nose and sore throat, but after about a week, the infection can develop into coughing bouts that last for a few minutes and are typically worse at night. Young babies may also make a distinctive 'whoop' or have difficulty breathing after a bout of coughing, though not all babies make this noise which means whooping cough can be hard to recognise.\n\n\"If anyone in your family is diagnosed with whooping cough, it's important they stay at home and do not go into work, school or nursery until 48 hours after starting antibiotics, or 3 weeks after symptoms start if they have not had antibiotics. This helps to prevent the spread of infection, especially to vulnerable groups, including infants. {"uri": "8118924955", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "10:14:09", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T10:14:09Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T10:13:46Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.886274516582489, "url": "https://www.mirror.co.uk/news/health/whooping-cough-symptoms--what-32769412", "title": "Whooping cough symptoms for parents to beware of as five babies die from disease", "body": "Babies are given multiple vaccines to protect against whooping cough\n\nBrit are being warned to watch out for whooping cough symptoms amid a worrying spike in cases that's seen five babies die.\n\nMore than 2,700 cases of whooping cough - also known as pertussis - have been reported across England so far in 2024. That's more than three times the amount recorded in the whole of last year. The worrying new figures released by the UK Heath Security Agency (UKHSA) show there were 2,793 cases reported to the end of March. This is compared with the 858 cases for the whole of 2023. The UKHSA confirmed that between January and the end of March, there have also been five infant deaths.\n\nWhooping cough is a highly contagious bacterial infection of the lungs and airways, says the NHS. It causes repeated coughing bouts that can last for two to three months or more, and can make babies and young children in particular very ill. Whooping cough is spread in the droplets of the coughs or sneezes of someone with the infection.\n\nSymptoms of whooping cough appear similar to those of a normal cold with \"a runny nose, red and watery eyes, a sore throat and a slightly raised temperature\" states the NHS. This is then followed by the key sign of coughs that begin a week later where there are bouts that last for a few minutes and are more common at night. The coughing may bring up thick mucus and could lead to vomiting.\n\n\"Between coughs, you or your child may gasp for breath - this may cause a 'whoop' sound, although not everyone has this,\" states the NHS. \"The strain of coughing can cause the face to become very red, and there may be some slight bleeding under the skin or in the eyes. Young children can sometimes briefly turn blue (cyanosis) if they have trouble breathing - this often looks worse than it is and their breathing should start again quickly.\n\n\"In very young babies, the cough may not be particularly noticeable, but there may be brief periods where they stop breathing. The bouts will eventually start to become less severe and less frequent over time, but it may be a few months before they stop completely.\"\n\nWho's at risk of whooping cough\n\nWhooping cough can affect people of any age, including:\n\nbabies and young children - young babies under 6 months of age are at a particularly increased risk of complications of whooping cough older children and adults - it tends to be less serious in these cases, but can still be unpleasant and frustrating people who've had whooping cough before - you're not immune to whooping cough if you've had it before, although it tends to be less severe the second time around people vaccinated against whooping cough as a child - protection from the whooping cough vaccine tends to wear off after a few years When to go to the GP showing whooping cough symptoms have a cough for more than three weeks have a cough that is particularly severe or is getting worse\n\nUKHSA consultant epidemiologist Dr Gayatri Amirthalingam said: \"Whooping cough can affect people of all ages but for very young babies it can be extremely serious. Our thoughts and condolences are with those families who have so tragically lost their baby.\" Dr Amirthalingam also said vaccination remains the best form of defence against the Victorian-era disease. {"uri": "8119274147", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "14:04:39", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T14:04:39Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T14:03:49Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8823529481887817, "url": "https://news.sky.com/story/what-is-whooping-cough-symptoms-parents-should-look-for-as-cases-rise-13132156", "title": "What is whooping cough? Symptoms parents should look for as cases rise", "body": "Whooping cough is sometimes called the \"100-day cough\" because of how long it can take to recover from. It is very contagious and can be extremely serious for young babies.\n\nWhooping cough cases have risen sharply in the UK, with doctors warning the infection is \"extremely serious\" for young babies.\n\nHere is what you need to know about the symptoms, vaccine and treatment.\n\nWhat is whooping cough?\n\nWhooping cough is sometimes called the \"100-day cough\" because of how long it can take to recover from.\n\nThe bacterial infection, also known as pertussis, affects the lungs and breathing tubes and causes bouts of coughing.\n\nIt is highly contagious and is spread in the droplets of coughs and sneezes.\n\nWhat are the symptoms of whooping cough?\n\nThe first symptoms are similar to that of a cold and may include:\n\n* runny nose\n\n* red and watery eyes\n\n* sore throat\n\n* slightly raised temperature\n\nIntense coughing bouts start about a week later, according to the NHS.\n\nThe bouts are typically more common at night and can last a few minutes.\n\nBetween coughs, you or your child may gasp for breath, causing a \"whoop\" sound. However, not everyone has this.\n\nThe coughing bouts may cause difficulty breathing, and young infants in particular may turn blue or grey.\n\n\"This often looks worse than it is and their breathing should start again quickly,\" according to NHS Inform.\n\nThe coughing may also bring up a thick mucus, which can make you vomit.\n\nThe bouts can also cause the face to become very red, particularly in adults.\n\nHow serious is whooping cough?\n\n\"Whooping cough can affect people of all ages but for very young babies it can be extremely serious,\" according to Dr Gayatri Amirthalingam, consultant epidemiologist at the UK Health Security Agency (UKHSA).\n\nBabies under 6 months old with whooping cough have a higher risk of problems including dehydration, breathing difficulties, pneumonia and seizures, according to the NHS.\n\nFive babies died of whooping cough between January and the end of March 2024.\n\nPeople who are infected with whooping cough can pass on the bacteria to others for two to three weeks.\n\n\"As a disease, whooping cough is as infectious as measles, and more infectious than COVID-19,\" said Dr Michael Head, senior research fellow in global health at the University of Southampton.\n\nRead more from Sky News:\n\nWhat is Strep A and what are the symptoms?\n\nWhat is RSV? Symptoms, complications and when to seek help\n\nHow common is whooping cough?\n\nHealth officials describe whooping cough as a \"cyclical disease\", meaning it peaks every three to five years, with the last big increase in 2016.\n\nThere were 2,793 cases reported to the end of March, according to provisional data from the UKHSA.\n\nThere were 1,319 cases reported in March alone, according to the provisional data. There were 556 cases recorded in January and 918 in February.\n\nHow can you protect yourself or your child from whooping cough?\n\nThe NHS recommends all pregnant women are vaccinated against whooping cough between 16 and 32 weeks.\n\nThis means the immunity is passed through the placenta to protect newborn babies in their first weeks of life.\n\nThe whooping cough vaccine is routinely given to babies as part of the 6-in-1 vaccine at eight, 12 and 16 weeks.\n\nIt is also part of the 4-in-1 pre-school booster given to children at 3 years and 4 months.\n\nDr Head said a drop in the number of people being vaccinated is a \"key factor\" in the increase in cases.\n\n\"It's vital that vulnerable groups such as babies and pregnant women are up to date with their recommended immunisations,\" he said.\n\nIf whooping cough is diagnosed within three weeks, antibiotics will be given to help stop it spreading to others.\n\nIf you've had whooping cough for longer than that, you are no longer contagious and do not need antibiotics, according to the NHS.\n\nIt is important to stay off school, work or nursery until 48 hours after starting antibiotics, or three weeks after symptoms started if you've not had antibiotics.\n\nThe NHS advises getting plenty of rest and fluids to treat the symptoms of whooping cough.\n\nParacetamol and ibuprofen may help - although they're not to be given at the same time to under-16s - but cough medicine does not help with this type of cough.\n\nWhat should you do if you or your child have symptoms?\n\nNHS advises you should ask for an urgent GP appointment or call NHS 111 if:\n\n* you have a baby under six months with whooping cough symptoms\n\n* you're pregnant and have been in contact with someone with whooping cough\n\n* you (or your child) has a weakened immune system and have been in contact with someone with whooping cough\n\n* you or your child has a very bad cough that is getting worse\n\nYou should call 999 or go to A&E Live a healthier, happier life with the free weekly Living Well newsletter.\n\nFive babies have died in the UK after being diagnosed with whooping cough, prompting a warning about the disease.\n\nAs of 2024, more than 2,700 cases of whooping cough have been reported across the UK. This is more than three times the figure recorded for all of last year.\n\nNew UK Health and Security Agency (UKHSA) figures show 2,793 cases were reported by the end of March.\n\nThis compares to 858 for all of 2023.\n\nUKHSA said there were five infant deaths between January and the end of March.\n\nUKHSA consultant epidemiologist Dr Gayatri Amirthalingam said: Whooping cough can affect people of all ages, but can be very serious in very young babies.\n\nOur thoughts and condolences are with the family who so tragically lost their baby.\n\nAccording to provisional data, 1,319 cases were reported in March alone.\n\nFive babies have died after being diagnosed with pertussis (PA) in the UK this year (PA archive)\n\nThe bacterial infection, also known as whooping cough or whooping cough, can be especially serious in very young children and, in very rare cases, can be fatal.\n\nThe numbers tend to increase every few years, and we believe the increase in social contact since the pandemic is now playing a part.\n\nAlways consult your doctor for an accurate diagnosis and treatment advice, and seek urgent help if you have signs of difficulty breathing.\n\nWhat does whooping cough sound like?\n\nWhile many coughs are caused by the common cold virus, whooping cough is a bacterial infection (the medical term for whooping cough).\n\nWhooping cough often presents as a normal cough or cold at first, but you may notice symptoms getting worse after a week or two, says Dr. Kathryn Basford, of Zava, an online doctor. While a typical cough may go away within a few weeks and feel mild, whooping cough can last much longer, even months.\n\nThe main difference to note between a mild cough and whooping cough is its intensity. Whooping cough involves a strong coughing sound, especially at night, and a high-pitched whooping cough, especially when the person has difficulty breathing. Vomiting, flushing, and difficulty breathing may also occur.\n\nAnd unlike a normal cough, whooping cough is much more contagious. If you have not yet been vaccinated against whooping cough, it is recommended that you see your doctor to get vaccinated. Early diagnosis and treatment are important to prevent complications and spread.\n\nHow do you catch whooping cough?\n\nWhooping cough is a highly contagious respiratory disease that spreads through the air through tiny droplets expelled when an infected person coughs or sneezes. Basford says these droplets can be inhaled by others nearby.\n\nBacteria can also remain on surfaces touched by an infected individual and can be transmitted indirectly by someone else touching the surface and then touching their eyes, nose, or mouth. That's why maintaining good respiratory hygiene, including washing your hands frequently and covering your mouth when coughing or sneezing, is important to prevent the spread.\n\nExperts urge pregnant women to get the whooping cough vaccine to protect their babies (PA) (PA archive)\n\nIs whooping cough contagious to adults?\n\nBasford explains: Whooping cough most commonly affects young children, but adults can also get it. Adults usually experience milder symptoms than children.\n\nCan whooping cough be serious?\n\nWhooping cough can certainly be serious, especially in young babies who have not yet been vaccinated. Young children, in particular, may have trouble breathing during a coughing fit, which can lead to complications such as pneumonia and, in some rare and serious cases, death, Basford warns. Even in adults, persistent coughing can be debilitating, causing sleep problems and making daily activities difficult.\n\nHow else can you prevent it?\n\nThe best way to avoid serious whooping cough is to get vaccinated. This vaccine is given to your baby as part of the routine NHS immunization schedule and then as part of the kindergarten booster immunization. Pregnant women should also be vaccinated to protect their babies after they are born. This is available for between 16 and 32 weeks.\n\nHow is whooping cough treated?\n\nTreatment for whooping cough depends on several factors, including age and how long the cough has lasted. Infants younger than 6 months are at higher risk for complications and often require hospitalization and special treatment, Basford says.\n\nIf diagnosed early (within 3 weeks), antibiotics may be helpful. Medication may not make you feel better any faster, but it plays an important role in stopping the spread. However, if it has been more than 3 weeks, antibiotics may not help because you are likely no longer contagious.\n\nFocus on symptom management. Get plenty of rest, stay hydrated, and use a cool mist humidifier to relieve coughing. Pain relievers such as ibuprofen or paracetamol may also help relieve discomfort.\n\nWhat Are The Main Benefits Of Comparing Car Insurance Quotes Online", "source": {"uri": "exbulletin.com", Some 51% of cases during this period were among those aged 15 and older.\n\nHealth officials describe whooping cough as a \"cyclical disease\", which means it peaks every few years. {"uri": "2024-05-350137948", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "11:14:49", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T11:14:49Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T11:13:09Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8627451062202454, "url": "https://www.itv.com/news/2024-05-09/whats-the-difference-between-whooping-cough-and-a-normal-cough", "title": "What's the difference between whooping cough and a normal cough?", "body": "New figures from the UK Health Security Agency (UKHSA) show 1,319 cases of the illness were detected in March, up from 918 in February and 556 in January. This compares to just 858 cases of whooping cough for the whole of 2023.\n\nFive infants are also known to have died in between January and March 2024.\n\nDr Michael Head, Senior Research Fellow in Global Health told ITV News these figures are significant - but not unprecedented.\n\n\"The data is definitely concerning,\" he said.\n\n\"We do see large outbreaks of whooping cough on a cycle every few years but the one we're seeing at the moment is big.\"\n\nBut what has caused this most recent spike in cases, and how simple is it to tell the difference between whooping cough and a regular cough?\n\nWhat is whooping cough?\n\nWhooping cough is a infection of the lungs and airways. It is caused by bacteria called Bordetella pertussis, and is very contagious.\n\nWhat are the symptoms?\n\nThe first signs of whooping cough are similar to those of a cold, such as a runny nose and a sore throat.\n\nSymptoms then progress after about a week into coughing bouts lasting a few minutes, which might get worse at night. Coughing can last for weeks or months.\n\nThose with the illness may make a 'whoop' sound, caused by gasping for breath between coughs - but this does not happen everyone with whooping cough.\n\nIt can cause breathing difficulties, with young infants turning blue or grey and adults becoming red in the face after coughing bouts.\n\nWhooping cough may also bring up thick mucus, which can cause vomiting.\n\nHow is it different to a normal cough?\n\nDr Head explained whooping cough is similar to other kinds of respiratory illness in the early stages.\n\nHe said: \"You'll have some kind of cough, and perhaps a runny nose or a fever. It is very, very hard to tell the difference at that stage between whooping cough and anything else.\n\n\"You can only really notice it's whooping cough later on in the presentation when that distinctive 'hoop' sound kicks in.\"\n\nGiven not everyone experiences this symptom, the illness is difficult to accurately diagnose without laboratory testing, according to Dr Head.\n\n\"That's another reason why it is hard to get outbreaks under control and why vaccination is vital,\" he said.\n\nHow serious is it?\n\nWhooping cough can lead to serious and sometimes deadly complications in babies and young children.\n\nAround a third of babies under the age of one with whooping cough will need hospital care.\n\nComplications can include apnea (life-threatening pauses in breathing), pneumonia, convulsions and encephalopathy (disease of the brain).\n\nTeenagers and adults can also experience complications, but they are generally less serious in those who are older.\n\nWhat has caused this recent outbreak?\n\nAccording to Dr Head, outbreaks of whooping cough happen every few years.\n\nHe said: \"The reasons are not totally clear but will be related to drops in vaccine uptake and probably waning of immunity in older children and younger adults.\n\n\"Once you get to that sort of nine or ten years of age, the immunity from the vaccines does start to wear off. Waning of immunity and any lower vaccine uptake does mean the vulnerable populations like children and pregnant women are more likely to be exposed.\"\n\nDr Head believes the time of year could explain the uptick in cases across the past few months.\n\n\"The reason why we see respiratory infections during the winter is that people will cluster indoors. If it's cold and wet, if you're indoors with little ventilation, the window shut, then there's many opportunities for that bacteria to spread,\" he said.\n\nHow is it treated?\n\nThose with severe whooping cough, or babies under 6 months with the illness, will need hospital treatment. It is treated with antibiotics.\n\nHow contagious is it?\n\nPeople with whooping cough are contagious from about six days after the start of cold-like symptoms to three weeks after the coughing starts.\n\nStarting antibiotics within three weeks of starting to cough can reduce the time someone is contagious for.\n\nDr Head explained whooping cough is about as contagious as measles, which is one of the most infectious diseases around.\n\n\"It has an R number of 15 to 17, so one infected person can potentially infect 15 to 17 other separable people,\" he said.\n\n\"It's also mostly in the upper respiratory tract meaning it is easier to spread because the bacteria are nearer the the the mouth and nose.\"\n\nIs there a vaccine?\n\nAll babies are offered a whooping cough vaccination as part of routine NHS vaccines. It is given as part of the six-in-one vaccine for babies at eight, 12, and 16 weeks old.\n\nIt's also administered in the four-in-one pre-school booster, for children aged three years and four months old.\n\nThe NHS recommends all pregnant women are vaccinated against whooping cough between 16 and 32 weeks.\n\nDr Head said: \"High vaccine coverage is vital to maintain each and every year to protect pregnant women and babies. Otherwise, we get outbreaks like we're seeing this year.\n\n\"If you are recommended for vaccine, then do take up the offer for you or your child.\"\n\nWhat should those worried about whooping cough do?\n\nAsk for an urgent GP appointment or call NHS 111 if:\n\nCall 999 or go to A&E if:\n\nVisit the NHS website for more advice.", "source": {"uri": "itv.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "ITV Some 51% of cases during this period were among those aged 15 and older.\n\nHealth officials describe whooping cough as a \"cyclical disease\", which means it peaks every few years. for pregnant women and children.\n\nDr Amirthalingam added: \"Vaccination remains the best defence against whooping cough and it is vital that pregnant women and young infants receive their vaccines at the right time.\"\n\nThe NHS recommends all pregnant women are vaccinated against whooping cough between 16 and 32 weeks.\n\nImmunity from the jab passes through the placenta to protect newborn offered at 12 weeks and the third is offered at 16 weeks.\n\nWhen children are three years and four months they will be offered the four-in-one pre-school booster, which protects against The UK Health Security Agency (UKHSA) reported 1,319 cases in England in March, after just over 900 in February, making the 2024 total nearly 2,800.\n\nThe last peak year, 2016, had 5,949 cases in England. The infection - known as the 100-day cough - can be particularly serious for babies and infants. Half of cases seen so far this year have been in the under-15s, with the highest rates in babies under three months of age.\n\nThe five babies who died this year were all under three months old. These are the first deaths since 2019.\n\nREAD: How to tell what insect has bitten or stung you - and when to be worried Some bites and stings can potentially be life-threatening, but there are ways to tell them apart\n\nREAD:Doctor says five simple letters could tell if you have skin cancer 17,500 people are diagnosed with melanoma skin cancers every year, and early diagnosis helps with the outcome\n\nUKHSA has said a steady decline in uptake of the vaccine in pregnant women and children and the very low numbers seen during the pandemic, as happened with other infections because of restrictions and public behaviour, were both factors.\n\nThe agency said a peak year was therefore overdue and urged families to come forward to get vaccinated if they had not already.\n\nBelow, PA news agency provides answers to frequently asked questions about the illness.\n\nWhat exactly is whooping cough?\n\nThis bacterial infection, also known as pertussis, targets the lungs and airways. Often referred to as the \"100-day cough\" due to the lengthy recovery period, whooping cough is highly contagious.\n\nCould you describe the symptoms of whooping cough?\n\nInitially, whooping cough presents cold-like symptoms such as a runny nose and sore throat. However, approximately a week later, it can progress into severe coughing fits that last several minutes and are often more intense at night.\n\nInfants may produce a characteristic \"whoop\" sound or struggle to breathe after coughing, although not all exhibit this symptom.\n\nWho does it affect the most?\n\nWhile individuals aged 15 and above account for 51% of diagnoses, the disease poses the greatest risk to babies. In the first quarter of the year, 108 infants younger than three months were diagnosed with whooping cough.\n\nWhat's the extent of the outbreak?\n\nFrom January to March, there were 2,793 reported cases, per UK Health Security Agency (UKHSA) data. March saw 1,319 cases alone, with 556 in January and 918 in February.\n\nHow does this stack up against previous years?\n\nThere were a total of 858 cases reported throughout the entirety of 2023.\n\nWhat's behind the surge in cases?\n\nExperts at the UK Health Security Agency (UKHSA) explain that whooping cough is known to be a \"cyclical disease\", with peaks occurring every three to five years. The most recent significant rise was observed in 2016.\n\nHowever, during the Covid-19 pandemic, instances of whooping cough were unusually low, likely due to reduced social interaction and adherence to social distancing guidelines. As a result, the current spike is considered \"overdue\", according to the UKHSA.\n\nThe agency also noted that the pandemic has contributed to \"reduced immunity in the population\". Additionally, vaccination rates have seen a decline over the past few years.\n\nHow can individuals safeguard themselves?\n\nExpectant mothers are being strongly encouraged to accept the whooping cough vaccine to convey immunity to their newborns, which should sustain until the infants are eligible for their own vaccinations. Parents are likewise being reminded to ensure their children receive vaccinations when invited.\n\nWhen is vaccination available?\n\nThe NHS advises that all pregnant women should receive the whooping cough vaccine between the 16th and 32nd weeks of pregnancy. At eight weeks old, babies are offered the six-in-one vaccine, which includes protection against whooping cough.\n\nThe second jab is offered at 12 weeks, followed by a third at 16 weeks. Once children reach the age of three years and four months, they are offered the four-in-one pre-school booster to protect against pertussis.\n\nSign up to our main daily newsletter here and get all {"uri": "8118823097", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "09:12:17", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T09:12:17Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T09:10:30Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8549019694328308, "url": "https://foreignaffairs.co.nz/2024/05/09/mil-osi-united-kingdom-whooping-cough-cases-continue-to-rise/", "title": "MIL-OSI United Kingdom: Whooping cough cases continue to rise", "body": "New data published today by the UK Health Security Agency (UKHSA) shows cases of whooping cough continue to increase with 1,319 cases confirmed in March.\n\nNew data published today by the UK Health Security Agency (UKHSA) shows cases of whooping cough continue to increase with 1,319 cases confirmed in March. This follows 556 cases in January and 918 in February, bringing the total number of cases in 2024 to 2,793.\n\nSadly, in the first quarter of 2024 (January - March), there have been five infant deaths. Young infants are at highest risk of severe complications and death from whooping cough. Updated estimates of vaccine effectiveness in pregnancy shows high levels of protection (92%) against infant death.\n\nDuring this quarter, while most cases (50.8%, 1420) were in those aged 15 years or older who usually get a mild illness, the rates of whooping cough remain highest in babies under 3 months of age.\n\nWhooping cough cases have been rising across England, as well as in many other countries, since December 2023 due to a combination of factors. Whooping cough is a cyclical disease that peaks every 3 to 5 years. The last cyclical increase occurred in 2016. However, in common with other diseases, cases fell to very low numbers during the pandemic due to restrictions and public behaviours. A peak year is therefore overdue. The impact of the pandemic also means there is reduced immunity in the population.\n\nUptake of vaccinations that protect against whooping cough have fallen in recent years across the country - in both the programme for pregnant women and the infant programme. Timely vaccination in pregnancy and in infancy are both important to protect vulnerable young babies from serious disease.\n\nDr Gayatri Amirthalingam, Consultant Epidemiologist at UK Health Security Agency, said:\n\nVaccination remains the best defence against whooping cough and it is vital that pregnant women and young infants receive their vaccines at the right time.\n\nPregnant women are offered a whooping cough vaccine in every pregnancy, ideally between 20 and 32 weeks. This passes protection to their baby in the womb so that they are protected from birth in the first months of their life when they are most vulnerable and before they can receive their own vaccines.\n\nAll babies are given three doses of the 6 in 1 jab at 8, 12 and 16 weeks of age to protect against whooping cough and other serious diseases such as diphtheria and polio with a pre-school booster offered at 3 years 4 months.\n\nWhooping cough can affect people of all ages but for very young babies it can be extremely serious. Our thoughts and condolences are with those families who have so tragically lost their baby.\n\nNHS National Medical Director, Professor Sir Stephen Powis, said:\n\nWith cases of whooping cough continuing to rise sharply across the country, and today's figures sadly showing five infant deaths, it is vital that families come forward to get the protection they need.\n\nIf you are pregnant and have not been vaccinated yet, or your child is not up-to-date with whooping cough or other routine vaccinations, please contact your GP as soon as possible, and if you or your child show symptoms ask for an urgent GP appointment or get help from NHS 111.\n\nWhooping cough, clinically known as pertussis, is a bacterial infection which affects the lungs. The first signs of infection are similar to a cold, such as a runny nose and sore throat, but after about a week, the infection can develop into coughing bouts that last for a few minutes and are typically worse at night. Young babies may also make a distinctive 'whoop' or have difficulty breathing after a bout of coughing, though not all babies make this noise which means whooping cough can be hard to recognise.\n\nIf anyone in your family is diagnosed with whooping cough, it's important they stay at home and do not go into work, school or nursery until 48 hours after starting antibiotics, or 3 weeks after symptoms start if they have not had antibiotics. This helps to prevent the spread of infection, especially to vulnerable groups, including infants. {"uri": "8119402018", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "15:34:22", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T15:34:22Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T15:33:47Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8509804010391235, "url": "https://www.mirror.co.uk/news/health/whooping-cough-you-need-know-32773435", "title": "Whooping cough: All you need to know as killer illness sweeps UK", "body": "Health officials have confirmed five babies in England have died\n\nCases of whooping cough in England have more than tripled in 2024 so far compared to the number of cases recorded last year.\n\nIt comes as health officials confirmed five babies, all under three months old, have died from the disease in England. The UK Health Security Agency (UKHSA) reported 1,319 cases in England in March, and just over 900 in February, making the 2024 total nearly 2,800.\n\nKnown more commonly as the '100-day cough', the infection can be particularly serious for babies and infants, and cases tend to peak every three to five years. With growing fears that figures will continue to rise this year, here is all you need to know about the illness and how it is treated.\n\nHistory of whooping cough Whooping cough, or pertussis, was discovered in 1906\n\nThe literal meaning of pertussis in Latin is \"a violent cough\" and it was first described in the Paris epidemic of 1578.\n\nBordetella pertussis, the bacteria that causes it, was discovered in 1906 and a vaccine was developed in the 1940s. In the 12 years before the NHS started offering all pregnant women a vaccine against it in 2012 there were 63 deaths in babies under one. In the next ten years there were 21 such deaths but they have tended to cluster in outbreak years. Experts say 2024 appears to be another one of those with five deaths recorded in just the first three months.\n\nWhy is it bad?\n\nWhooping cough is as infectious as measles and more infectious than Covid-19.\n\nIt spreads because of its long period when patients with it can be infectious of up to three weeks. It is also known as the \"100-day cough\" because of how long it can take to recover from. Half of cases seen so far this year have been in the under-15s, with the highest rates in babies under three months of age for whom it is particularly dangerous.\n\nThe NHS says babies under six months old are at increased risk of problems such as dehydration, breathing difficulties, pneumonia and seizures. In older children and adults violent coughing fits can cause sore ribs and even lead to a hernia or ear infections\n\nDr Michael Head, global health expert at Southampton University, said: \"Whooping cough can have a very long infectious period of around two to three weeks. \"Thus there may be plenty of opportunities for people to mix, transmission to occur, and for there to be an infection of a vulnerable individual, such as a baby.\"\n\nWhat is causing the outbreak?\n\nWhooping cough is considered a cyclical disease which peaks every three to five years.\n\nThe last big increase was seen in 2016 before the Covid-19 pandemic reduced social contacts so we are due a bounce back. Vaccination uptake has also dropped with rates among pregnant women falling from more than 70% in 2017 to 58% in 2023.\n\nThe number of two-year-olds who completed their routine six-in-one vaccinations, which includes protection against whooping cough, was 92.9% in 2023 compared with 96.3% in 2014. Infections began to rise a little from last summer but have really surged since December 2023.\n\nDr Head said: \"Outbreaks can occur in cycles so we tend to see these rises in cases every few years. The reasons for that are not fully understood but population-level waning immunity is likely to contribute to that, hence why a high coverage of vaccination is so important.\"\n\nWhat should we look out for? The infection can develop into coughing bouts that last for a few minutes\n\nThe UKHSA says the first signs of whooping cough infection are similar to a cold such as a runny nose and sore throat.\n\nHowever, after about a week the infection can develop into coughing bouts that last for a few minutes that are typically worse at night. Young babies may also make a distinctive 'whoop' - a gasp for breath between coughs. But some adults and young babies do not make this noise which can make the condition harder to recognise.\n\nThe NHS says some may bring up a thick mucus, which can make you vomit, while adults may be left red in the face from coughing. Professor Sir Stephen Powis, NHS England Medical Director, said: \"If you or your child show symptoms ask for an urgent GP appointment or get help from NHS 111.\"\n\nThe UKHSA warns that if anyone in your family is diagnosed with whooping cough they should stay home and not attend work, school or nursery until 48 hours after starting antibiotics, or 3 weeks after symptoms start if they have not had antibiotics.\n\nWho can get vaccinated and when?\n\nHealth chiefs are now highlighting the dangers of whooping cough to try to reverse drops in vaccine uptake against it.\n\nThe NHS recommends all pregnant women are vaccinated against pertussis between 16 and 32 weeks. Immunity from the jab passes through the placenta to protect newborn babies in their first weeks of life. When a baby is eight weeks old they are offered the six-in-one vaccine which includes immunisation against whooping cough.\n\nThe second dose of the vaccine is offered at 12 weeks and the third is offered at 16 weeks. When children are three years and four months they will be offered the four-in-one pre-school booster which protects against pertussis.", "source": {"uri": "mirror.co.uk", "dataType": "news", "title": The UKHSA worries this means it may be a 'bumper' year for the infection after the last peak was in 2016 with 5,949 cases.\n\nThe NHS describes whooping cough (also known as pertussis) as a bacterial infection of the lungs and breathing tubes. It can cause serious issues, especially among children and babies, which is why the health service recommends vaccinating against it.\n\n\"Vaccination remains the best defence against whooping cough and it is vital that pregnant women and young infants receive their vaccines at the right time,\" Dr Gayatri Amirthalingam of the UKHSA told the BBC.\n\n\"Whooping cough can affect people of all ages, but for very young babies it can be extremely serious. Our thoughts and condolences are with those families who have so tragically lost their baby.\"\n\nThe UKHSA has seen a steady decline in children completing their vaccinations over the past decade.\n\nIn 2023, just 92.9% of two-year-olds had completed their six-in-one vaccinations, which protects against whooping cough, compared to 96.3% of the same age group in 2014. It added that the maternal pertussis vaccine, which is offered to pregnant women, dropped from 70% in 2017 to 58% in 2023.\n\nThis phenomenon could be described as 'vaccine hesitation', with a growing number of people hesitant to vaccinate their children.\n\nWhat is vaccine hesitancy?\n\n\"Vaccine hesitancy has been on the rise in recent years due to a confluence of factors,\" Dr Joseph Ambani tells Yahoo UK. \"Misinformation spread through various channels, including social media, has fuelled scepticism and eroded trust in vaccines and the healthcare system.\n\n\"Additionally, a growing emphasis on individual autonomy and distrust in authority figures has contributed to a culture of vaccine hesitancy. Cultural, religious, and ideological beliefs also play a significant role, influencing perceptions of vaccine necessity and safety.\"\n\nHe adds that addressing vaccine hesitancy requires a 'multi-faceted approach' that involves providing accurate information and promoting the importance of vaccination for individual and community health.\n\n\"Among parents, concerns about vaccine ingredients, potential side effects, and perceived conflicts of interest within the medical community can contribute to hesitancy,\" he adds. \"The decline in infant vaccination rates against whooping cough can be attributed to a combination of these factors, compounded by the dissemination of misinformation through social media platforms and anti-vaccine advocacy groups.\"\n\nSigns of whooping cough\n\nThe first signs of whooping cough are similar to a cold, like a runny nose and sore throat. However after around a week, the infection can develop into coughing bouts that last for a few minutes and are typically worse at night, according to the UKHSA. Young babies may also make a distinctive 'whoop' sound - hence the name - or have difficulty breathing after coughing. But it's important to note not all babies make this noise, meaning whooping cough can be hard to recognise.\n\nIt is a highly contagious disease and infants are particularly vulnerable to it thanks to their underdeveloped immune systems.\n\nWhy vaccinations against whooping cough is important\n\n\"Complications of whooping cough in infants can be severe and even life-threatening, including pneumonia, seizures, apnoea, and brain damage,\" Dr Ambani warns. \"Tragically, infants under the age of one year are at the highest risk of mortality from pertussis infections.\"\n\nThis is why he urges parents to vaccinate their children against the condition, both to protect the child and to contribute towards herd immunity.\n\n\"[Herd immunity] is crucial for protecting vulnerable individuals who cannot be vaccinated, such as newborns too young to receive the vaccine and individuals with certain medical conditions,\" Dr Ambani says. \"By ensuring high vaccination coverage rates in the population, we create a protective barrier against the spread of whooping cough, safeguarding the health of our communities.\"\n\nTo find out more about whooping cough, visit the NHS information page here.
