{"uri": "8119808449", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "21:21:36", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T21:21:36Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T21:21:12Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8509804010391235, "url": "https://www.mirror.co.uk/news/health/uks-worst-whooping-cough-outbreak-32776054", "title": "Worst whooping cough outbreak for 40 years 'could be start of new epidemic'", "body": "The UK could be facing its worst whooping cough outbreak in 40 years\n\nFamilies were last night urged to get whooping cough jabs if not already protected after the deaths of five babies from the disease sparked fears of further tragedies.\n\nExperts are warning we could be facing the biggest outbreak in 40 years unless action is urgently taken. Parents were also told to look out for symptoms in children, including a runny nose, sore throat and a \"whooping\" noise when coughing and seek urgent medical help.\n\nThe UK Health Security Agency reported 1,319 cases in England during March, which was up from 918 in February and 556 in January. There were just 858 cases of the \u00ad\"100-day cough\", which can also cause brain damage, in the whole of last year.\n\nA fall in \u00advaccinations has been blamed for a national outbreak since the turn of the year - along with \u00adweakened immunity due to a lack of social mixing in lockdowns. Latest data shows uptake of the vaccine offered to all pregnant women to protect newborns plummeted from more than 70% in 2017 to 58% last year.\n\nParents are being urged to get the jab for their children (stock image) Preventable\n\nThe number of two-year-olds who had routine six-in-one jabs, which includes whooping cough protection, fell from 96.3% in 2014 to 92.9% in 2023. The babies who died of the disease, officially called pertussis, were all under three months old and they fell ill in the first three months of this year.\n\nNHS England's Medical Director Professor Sir Stephen Powis said: \"With cases of whooping cough continuing to rise sharply across the country, and today's figures sadly showing five infant deaths, it is vital that families come forward to get the protection they need. If you are pregnant and have not been vaccinated yet, or your child is not up to date with whooping cough or other \u00advaccinations, please contact your GP as soon as possible.\"\n\nBath University's Professor Andrew Preston, who has investigated the bacteria that cause whooping cough, added: \"It is extremely sad news to hear of the deaths of five babies, especially from a vaccine-preventable disease. While no vaccine is perfect, pertussis vaccination of mums during pregnancy and of infants protects the babies and infants who are the most vulnerable to this serious and \u00adsometimes fatal disease.\n\n\"Vaccination rates in infants have declined compared to \u00adpre-pandemic levels. Uptake of the maternal booster also dropped. This results in many more young babies and infants \u00adsusceptible to infection. It is tragic to see babies dying and with cases on the rise it is vital for vaccination rates to recover.\"\n\nRoyal College of GPs chair Professor Kamila Hawthorne said: \"We would urge all parents to check their children's vaccinations, and their own, are up to date, and if they're not, to make an appointment as soon as possible. It is also important for pregnant mothers to check their vaccination status and get vaccinated if they have not been immunised.\n\n\"If you, or a member of your family, does exhibit symptoms of whooping cough, especially a heavy wheezing cough that disrupts sleep, or a change in the colour of the face, then seek medical assistance.\"\n\nEmerging infectious diseases expert Professor Paul Hunter, of the World Health Organisation and the University of East Anglia, said: \"This current year looks like we may be seeing more cases than we have seen in any of the last 40 years. The infection can affect anyone who is not vaccinated and even some who are.\n\n\"However, the main risk of death or severe long-term complications is seen in young children, especially those under three months old. It is this group that are most at risk of death and longer-term \u00adproblems such as brain damage.\" Consultant epidemiologist at UKHSA Dr Gayatri Amirthalingam added: \"Whooping cough can affect people of all ages but for very young babies it can be extremely serious. Our thoughts and condolences are with those families who have so tragically lost their baby.\"\n\nNot all babies make the whooping sound when coughing, making the disease harder to spot. Other symptoms include coughing bouts that last for a few minutes and are typically worse at night. They could be followed by difficulty in breathing.\n\nThe last peak year in 2016 had 5,949 cases in England but health chiefs fear this year will be worse. In the first three months of 2024 there have been 2,793 cases.\n\nProf Hunter said other factors making the UK vulnerable includes the fact children unvaccinated by their parents due to the MMR jab scare sparked by discredited academic Andrew Wakefield are now in their early 20s and could drive population spread.\n\nSince the whooping cough pregnancy vaccine was introduced in 2013 there have been 26 deaths in babies too young to be protected by infant jabs. Half of cases so far this year have been under 15, with the highest rates in those under three months.\n\nMy month-old girl spent 10 days in coma Kerry Pearson, 26, with her daughter Baby Polly Deehy She is now awake and breathing independently\n\nA mum whose month-old girl spent 10 days in a coma with whooping cough is urging pregnant women to have the jab. Polly Deehy had a rattly cough and laboured breathing at two weeks. After four days coughing at home last month she suddenly turned blue and parents Kerry Pearson, 26 and Jack Deehy, 29, rushed her to Darent Valley Hospital, in Dartford, Kent.\n\nShe got worse and was moved to St Mary's Hospital in London where she was diagnosed with the condition and put on a ventilator. Polly is now awake and breathing independently.\n\nKerry said: \"I've never been more relieved. It was the best thing ever. There's no evidence of any lasting problems, but she might have a bit of weakness in her lungs. Time will tell. I just want people to be aware. You should have the whooping cough vaccine when you're pregnant. If you're not offered it then please demand it.\"\n\nOne-month-old Rosie Robson spent two weeks in Newcastle's Royal Victoria Infirmary\n\nThis was echoed by Molly Pickering, 25, of Co Durham, whose one-month-old Rosie Robson had two weeks in Newcastle's Royal Victoria Infirmary, including time in intensive care, with the condition. Molly said she recalled being offered the vaccine while pregnant but did not think there was enough information about how awful the illness can be. She said: \"At the hospital, the woman who was looking after Rosie said they had seen nothing like this [the number of whooping cough cases].\"\n\nWhooping cough Q&A\n\nQ What should we look out for?\n\nA The UKHSA says the first signs of whooping cough infection are similar to a cold such as a runny\n\nnose and sore throat. However, after about a week, it can develop into coughing bouts lasting minutes that are worse at night. These rapid, violent and uncontrolled coughing fits can last for 10 weeks.\n\nYoung babies may make a distinctive \"whoop\" - a gasp for breath between coughs. Some babies do not cough but turn blue or struggle to breathe. And some sufferers bring up a thick mucus, which can make them vomit.\n\nProf Sir Stephen Powis, NHS England Medical Director, said: \"If you or your child show symptoms ask for an urgent GP appointment or get help from NHS 111.\" Anyone diagnosed with whooping cough should stay home and not attend work, school or nursery until 48 hours after starting antibiotics.\n\nQ Why is whooping cough so bad?\n\nA Whooping cough spreads because patients with it can be infectious of up to three weeks. It is also known as the \"100-day cough\" because of how long it can take to recover from.\n\nHalf of cases this year have been in the under-15s, with the highest rates in babies under three months of age, for whom it is particularly dangerous. The NHS says babies under six months are at increased risk of dehydration, breathing difficulties, pneumonia and seizures.\n\nQ What is causing the current UK outbreak?\n\nA Whooping cough is a cyclical disease which peaks every three to five years. Infections have surged since December 2023. The last big increase was in 2016 before the pandemic reduced social contacts so we are due a bounce back.\n\nVaccination uptake fell with rates among pregnant women down from more than 70% in 2017 to 58% in 2023. The number of two-year-olds who completed their routine six-in-one vaccinations, which includes protection against whooping cough, was 92.9% in 2023 compared with 96.3% in 2014.\n\nQ Who can get vaccinated and when?\n\nAThe NHS recommends pregnant women are vaccinated between 16 and 32 weeks. Immunity from the jab passes through the placenta to protect babies in their first weeks. When a baby is eight weeks old, they are offered the six-in-one vaccine which protects against whooping cough. {"uri": "8119339069", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "14:48:35", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T14:48:35Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T14:48:11Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8470588326454163, "url": "https://www.irishnews.com/life/whooping-cough-what-is-it-and-how-do-you-catch-it-AKGVPOCJKJHKRIQIUIRNIRFHFM/", "title": "Whooping cough: What is it and how do you catch it?", "body": "As experts warn of rising case of whooping cough, a doctor explains why preventing it is so important.\n\nWhooping cough has been on the rise. The bacterial infection, also known as pertussis, affects the lungs and breathing tubes.\n\nThe Public Health Agency (PHA) has issued an appeal after a 'significant' rise in cases in Northern Ireland. There have been 769 confirmed cases of whooping cough to date in Northern Ireland compared with just two between 2021 and 2023.\n\nThe PHA is urging pregnant woman and the parents of young children to book an appointment for the pertussis vaccine.\n\nNumbers do tend to go up every few years, and it's believed the rise in social contact following the pandemic is playing a part right now.\n\nSo, what makes whooping cough different from regular coughs, and what do you need to know?\n\nWhile many coughs are caused by the common cold virus, whooping cough is a bacterial infection (known medically as pertussis)\n\nThe bacterial infection, also known as pertussis, affects the lungs and breathing tubes. Whooping cough is sometimes called the '100-day cough' because of how long it can take to recover from it, and it spreads very easily.\n\nThe first signs of whooping cough are similar to a cold and can include a runny nose and sore throat, but after about a week, the infection can develop into coughing bouts that last for a few minutes and are typically worse at night.\n\nYoung babies may also make a distinctive \"whoop\" or have difficulty breathing after a bout of coughing, though not all babies make this noise.\n\n\"Whooping cough often appears as a normal cough or cold at first, however you may notice symptoms intensifying after a week or two,\" says Dr Kathryn Basford from online doctor, Zava. \"While a typical cough clears up in a few weeks and feels mild, whooping cough can linger for much longer, even up to a few months.\n\n\"The key difference to look out for between a mild cough and whooping cough is the intensity. Whooping cough comes in strong coughing fits, especially at night, and most notably includes a high-pitched 'whoop' as you struggle to breathe. It can even lead to vomiting, a bright red face, and difficulty breathing.\n\n\"And unlike a regular cough, whooping cough is much more contagious. If you haven't received a vaccination for whooping cough, it's definitely worth seeing a doctor to explore getting one. Early diagnosis and treatment are crucial to prevent complications and spreading.\"\n\n\"Whooping cough is a highly contagious respiratory illness, meaning it spreads through the air via tiny droplets expelled when an infected person coughs or sneezes. These droplets can be inhaled by others nearby,\" says Basford.\n\n\"Additionally, the bacteria can linger on surfaces touched by an infected individual, creating indirect transmission if someone else touches the surface and then touches their eyes, nose, or mouth. This is why frequent hand washing and maintaining good respiratory hygiene, like covering your mouth when you cough or sneeze, are crucial in preventing the spread.\"\n\nBasford explains: \"While whooping cough is most commonly associated with young children, it is still possible for adults to catch it. Adults typically experience milder symptoms than children.\"\n\n\"Whooping cough can definitely be serious, especially for young babies who haven't yet been vaccinated. Younger children especially can struggle to breathe during coughing fits, leading to complications like pneumonia and in some rarer, more severe cases, death,\" Basford cautions. \"Even in adults, the constant coughing can be debilitating, causing sleep problems and making daily activities tough.\"\n\nThere is a whooping cough vaccine. Dr Basford says: \"The best way to avoid a serious whooping cough is with vaccination - the vaccine is included in the routine NHS vaccination schedule and is given to babies and then as part of the pre-school boosters. Pregnant women should also receive the vaccination to protect their baby once it is born; this is given between 16 and 32 weeks.\"\n\n\"Treating whooping cough depends on a few factors, like your age and how long you've had it. Infants under six months are at greater risk of complications and often require hospitalisation and specific care,\" says Basford.\n\n\"If you're diagnosed early (within three weeks), antibiotics can help. Medication won't necessarily make you feel better faster, but it plays a crucial role in stopping the spread. However, if it's been over three weeks, antibiotics may not help as much as you're likely to no longer be contagious.\n\n\"The focus is on managing symptoms: getting plenty of rest, staying hydrated, and using a cool mist humidifier to ease the cough. Painkillers such as ibuprofen or paracetamol can also help with discomfort.\"", "source": {"uri": "irishnews.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "The Irish News"}, "authors": [], "concepts": [{"uri": their recovery.\n\nMedics recommend focusing on managing symptoms by getting plenty of rest, staying hydrated, and using a cool mist humidifier to ease the cough.\n\nPainkillers such as ibuprofen or paracetamol can also help with discomfort.\n\nCan it be prevented?\n\nBecause whooping cough is so infectious, experts recommend self-isolating for at least five days while antibiotic treatment kicks in, if you suspect you have the illness.\n\nThe most effective preventable measure, however, is to get vaccinated.\n\nYour browser does not support iframes.\n\nThe 6-in-1 vaccine, given to babies at also encouraged to get the vaccine to protect their baby from catching the infection in the first few weeks of their life.\n\nHowever, uptake of the 6-in-1 vaccine dropped to a record low of 92.6 per cent in 2023, while the 4-in-1 jab hit 83.3, also the lowest level ever logged, according to NHS England data going back to 2010.\n\nMeanwhile, just 61.5 per cent of expectant mothers had the whooping cough is similar to other kinds of respiratory illness in the early stages.\n\nDr Head said: 'You'll have some kind of cough, and perhaps a runny nose or a fever. It is very, very hard to tell the difference at that stage between whooping cough and anything else.\n\n'You can only really notice it's whooping cough later on in the presentation when that distinctive 'hoop' sound kicks in'.\n\nGiven not everyone experiences this symptom, the illness is difficult to accurately diagnose without laboratory testing, he added.\n\nYour browser does not It is usually self-limiting but can cause severe illness and death, particularly in young unimmunised children.\n\n\"The infection is spread by respiratory droplets, either directly between people or through contaminated items. Babies under one year of age are most at risk from whooping cough.\n\n\"Whooping cough can be prevented with immunisation which is given to infants, younger children and pregnant women. The whooping cough vaccine is offered to pregnant women to help protect their baby against the infection.\n\n\"Getting immunised during pregnancy is the best way to protect the baby in the first few vulnerable weeks of their life until they're old enough to have the routine immunisation at eight weeks of age.\"\n\nAn increase in laboratory-confirmed pertussis cases was observed in Scotland in late 2023.\n\nOf the 73 cases reported in 2023, the majority were reported in the final quarter of the year.\n\nFor comparison, in 2022 and 2021 there were three and four cases of pertussis reported, respectively. Whooping cough is a cyclical disease that peaks every 3 to 5 years.\n\n\"The last cyclical increase occurred in 2016. However, in common with other diseases, cases fell to very low numbers during the pandemic due to restrictions and public behaviours. A peak year is therefore overdue. {"uri": "2024-05-350094683", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "10:36:54", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T10:36:54Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T10:24:20Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8313725590705872, "url": "https://uk.news.yahoo.com/whooping-cough-symptoms-deadly-102420362.html", "title": "What is whooping cough, what are the symptoms and how deadly is it?", "body": "The name whooping cough might conjure up images of Victorian England, but the disease is currently having a resurgence - with five baby deaths.\n\nMore than 2,700 whooping cough cases have been reported across England so far in 2024 - more than three times the amount recorded in the whole of last year.\n\nUK Health Security Agency (UKHSA) figures show there were 2,793 cases reported to the end of March. This is compared with the 858 cases for the whole of 2023.\n\nBetween July and the end of November 2023, there were 1,141 suspected cases of whooping cough in England and Wales, as reported by clinicians, nearly 700 more than the equivalent timeframes in 2021 and 2022.\n\nThe disease has spread across Europe. The European Centre for Disease Prevention and Control said that the bloc had seen more cases in the first three months of 2024 than an average year between 2012 and 2019.\n\nIt confirmed on Wednesday there had been 11 infant deaths and eight deaths among older adults on the continent.\n\nSo what is going on?\n\nWhooping cough is a cyclical disease that peaks every 3-5 years. The last cyclical increase occurred in 2016, with a major outbreak last seen in 2012.\n\nHealth officials said that the current deadly outbreak is \"overdue\" following suppression of the spread of disease during the pandemic due to lockdown and other restrictions.\n\nThe UK Health Security Agency (UKHSA) said \"the impact of the pandemic also means there is reduced immunity in the population\".\n\nWhat is whooping cough?\n\nThe name is given to a bacterial chest infection which can last for up to 100 days. \"It's highly contagious and it's spread via respiratory droplets,\" says Dr Safia Debar, executive health GP at Mayo Clinic Healthcare in London.\n\nThe illness progresses through three stages, the first being largely similar to a common cold with symptoms such as a runny nose, sore eyes and sneezing. After two weeks, infected individuals begin to develop bouts of intense coughing which can last for several minutes, sometimes followed by an audible whoop or gasp for breath between coughs. Young children may turn blue or grey due to difficulty breathing while adults might become red in the face.\n\nSufferers can then be left with a chronic cough which remains for several weeks.\n\nDebar recommends that anyone with a persistent cough which has lasted for more than two weeks, should see a doctor.\n\n\"Particularly if you also have symptoms like lethargy and fever, or you've been in contact with somebody with whooping cough, then definitely seek medical attention,\" she says. \"Without treatment, people are infectious for three weeks, but with antibiotics, you're not infectious after five days.\"\n\nHow serious is whooping cough?\n\nThe illness is most dangerous to newborn babies and infants. Most children under six months require hospitalisation after contracting whooping cough due to the risks of brain swelling and convulsions, and 1-3 per cent of those under three months die from the illness.\n\n\"Babies are vulnerable because their immune systems haven't fully developed,\" explains Dr Dipender Gill, a medical doctor and researcher at Imperial College London. \"It's quite prevalent in school-aged children as well, so those who are 11 to 18 years old.\"\n\nIn teenagers and adults, serious cases of whooping cough can lead to pneumonia, fainting and even rib fractures due to the intensity of the coughing fits. However, Gill says it tends to be the elderly and those with compromised immune systems who are most vulnerable.\n\n\"The whole population is susceptible to getting the infection,\" says Gill. \"But I think it can lead to particularly severe symptoms in elderly people, because as you age, your immune system becomes more impaired or if your immune system was impaired for other reasons such as cancer treatment or transplantation and immunosuppressive therapy.\"\n\nHow can you protect yourself and others?\n\nBecause whooping cough is so infectious, Debar recommends self-isolating if your doctor suspects that you have the illness, at least for five days while antibiotic treatment kicks in.\n\nHowever, the most effective preventable measure is to get vaccinated. Babies at eight, 12 and 16 weeks old are eligible for an immunisation to whooping cough on the NHS along with other vaccines. An additional booster shot can be given when the child is three.\n\nExperts are particularly keen to raise awareness that the whooping cough vaccine is now recommended for pregnant women.\n\n\"Newborns and babies in the first few months of life are more likely to get severe disease, [and] this is almost entirely preventable if the mother is vaccinated in pregnancy and her protective antibodies can then reach the baby through the placenta, and protect [it] until the baby gets its own vaccines,\" says Beate Kampmann, professor of paediatric infection & immunity at the London School of Hygiene and Tropical Medicine.\n\nKampmann recommends checking your vaccine records in case you missed receiving this jab. It is also possible for concerned older individuals to receive a booster vaccine for whooping cough.\n\nHow is it treated?\n\nDebar says that doctors will typically do a throat swab to see whether someone is infected with the Bordetella pertussis bacterium and if so, prescribe antibiotics.\n\n\"Antibiotics are given on a case-by-case basis,\" she says. \"After the first three weeks, the infection tends to wane and sometimes if you're just left with the chronic cough, it could be that you do not need antibiotics.\"\n\nShe says that once any fever has passed, it's not necessary to remain in bed the whole time, and instead there are certain remedies which can be taken to try and alleviate the coughing.\n\n\"You want to strengthen your immune system,\" says Debar. \"Some people will use cough remedies, we find that they don't really work. But things like saline sprays can sometimes provide symptomatic relief. I love natural remedies, so ginger, turmeric, all things to strengthen immunity.\n\n\"The coughing can be worse at night so you want the environment around you to be as moist as possible. So, use a humidifier, make sure the temperature is appropriate, and make sure you're well hydrated. Maybe try an extra pillow if that helps.\"\n\nWhy is whooping cough more common now?\n\nThe last major outbreak in England was in 2012 when 14 deaths were reported in one year.\n\nSince then, vaccinations have been introduced, with all the deaths occurring in those too young for vaccination.\n\nThe whooping cough vaccine is routinely given as part of the: 6-in-1 vaccine - for babies at 8, 12 and 16 weeks.\n\nIn 2012, vaccination of pregnant women was introduced, to protect the youngest babies. However, vaccination rates have been falling in recent years.\n\nExperts are concerned that the current outbreak is being fuelled by a combination of factors: vaccine hesitancy, suppression of disease linked to social distancing and reduced \"population immunity\" as a result.\n\nProfessor Paul Hunter, professor of medicine at the University of East Anglia (UEA), said the disease was much more common in the last century until vaccines were introduced\n\n\"This current year looks like we may see more cases than we have seen in any of the last 40 years,\" he said.\n\nProf Hunter said: \"The infection can affect anyone who is not vaccinated and even some that are. However, the main risk of death or severe long-term complications is seen in young children, especially those under three months old.\n\n\"It is this age group that is most at risk of death and developing longer-term problems such as brain damage.\n\n\"The problem is that this age group is too young for the vaccine in most circumstances.\n\n\"That is why we offer vaccines to pregnant women. Not to protect them but to protect their babies during the riskiest first months of life.\n\n\"Vaccine uptake in pregnant women has been falling quite markedly in recent years.\"", "source": {"uri": more than three times the number recorded in the whole of last year.\n\nNew UK Health Security Agency (UKHSA) figures show there were 2,793 cases reported to the end of March.\n\nThat compares to 858 cases for the whole of 2023.\n\nThe UKHSA said between January and the end of March, there have been five infant deaths.\n\nUKHSA consultant epidemiologist Dr Gayatri Amirthalingam said: \"Whooping cough can affect people of all ages, but for very young babies it can be extremely serious.\n\n\"Our thoughts and condolences are with those families who have so tragically lost their baby.\"\n\nIn March alone, some 1,319 cases were reported, according to the provisional data.\n\nAlso known as pertussis, or the \"hundred-day cough\", the bacterial infection can be particularly serious for very young children, for whom it can even be fatal in very rare cases.\n\nNumbers do tend to go up every few years, and it's believed the rise in social contact following the pandemic is playing a part right now.\n\nAlways consult your doctor for an accurate diagnosis and treatment advice, and seek urgent help if someone is experiencing any signs of breathing difficulties.\n\nWhile many coughs are caused by the common cold virus, whooping cough is a bacterial infection (known medically as pertussis).\n\n\"Whooping cough often appears as a normal cough or cold at first, however you may notice symptoms intensifying after a week or two,\" says Dr Kathryn Basford from online doctor, Zava. \"While a typical cough clears up in a few weeks and feels mild, whooping cough can linger for much longer, even up to a few months.\n\n\"The key difference to look out for between a mild cough and whooping cough is the intensity. Whooping cough comes in strong coughing fits, especially at night, and most notably includes a high-pitched 'whoop' as you struggle to breathe. It can even lead to vomiting, a bright red face, and difficulty breathing.\n\n\"And unlike a regular cough, whooping cough is much more contagious. If you haven't received a vaccination for whooping cough, it's definitely worth seeing a doctor to explore getting one. Early diagnosis and treatment are crucial to prevent complications and spreading.\"\n\n\"Whooping cough is a highly contagious respiratory illness, meaning it spreads through the air via tiny droplets expelled when an infected person coughs or sneezes. These droplets can be inhaled by others nearby,\" says Basford.\n\n\"Additionally, the bacteria can linger on surfaces touched by an infected individual, creating indirect transmission if someone else touches the surface and then touches their eyes, nose, or mouth. This is why frequent hand washing and maintaining good respiratory hygiene, like covering your mouth when you cough or sneeze, are crucial in preventing the spread.\"\n\nBasford explains: \"While whooping cough is most commonly associated with young children, it is still possible for adults to catch it. Adults typically experience milder symptoms than children.\"\n\n\"Whooping cough can definitely be serious, especially for young babies who haven't yet been vaccinated. Younger children especially can struggle to breathe during coughing fits, leading to complications like pneumonia and in some rarer, more severe cases, death,\" Basford cautions. \"Even in adults, the constant coughing can be debilitating, causing sleep problems and making daily activities tough.\"\n\n\"The best way to avoid a serious whooping cough is with vaccination - the vaccine is included in the routine NHS vaccination schedule and is given to babies and then as part of the pre-school boosters. Pregnant women should also receive the vaccination to protect their baby once it is born; this is given between 16 and 32 weeks.\"\n\n\"Treating whooping cough depends on a few factors, like your age and how long you've had it. Infants under six months are at greater risk of complications and often require hospitalisation and specific care,\" says Basford.\n\n\"If you're diagnosed early (within three weeks), antibiotics can help. Medication won't necessarily make you feel better faster, but it plays a crucial role in stopping the spread. However, if it's been over three weeks, antibiotics may not help as much as you're likely to no longer be contagious.\n\n\"The focus is on managing symptoms: getting plenty of rest, staying hydrated, and using a cool mist humidifier to ease the cough. Painkillers such as ibuprofen or paracetamol can also help with discomfort.\"", "source": {"uri": "independent.co.uk", "dataType": "news", "title": "The Independent"}, "authors": [], "concepts": Other signs of whooping cough include bringing up a thick mucus that can cause vomiting and becoming red in the face\n\nYour browser does not support iframes.\n\nHealth officials warned the infection is initially difficult to tell apart from a cold, with the first signs typically being a runny nose and sore throat.\n\nBut around a week later, sufferers may develop coughing bouts which last minutes, struggle to breathe after coughing and make a 'whoop' sound between coughs.\n\nOther signs of pertussis, as it is medically known, include bringing up a thick mucus that can cause vomiting and becoming red in the face.\n\nUKHSA surveillance figures show there were 2,793 cases reported this year to the end of March.\n\nWhat is whooping cough?\n\nWhooping cough is caused by the pertussis bacteria and is spread by coughing and sneezing.\n\nThe infection is initially difficult to tell apart from a cold, as the first signs are a runny nose and sore throat.\n\nBut around a week later, sufferers may develop coughing bouts that last minutes, struggle to breathe after coughing and make a 'whoop' sound between coughs.\n\nOther signs of whooping cough include bringing up a thick mucus that can cause vomiting and becoming red in the face.\n\nSufferers are infectious from around six days after cold-like symptoms develop to three weeks after their cough starts.\n\nDoctors dish out antibiotics as treatment if the whooping cough is detected within three weeks. However, if a person has been infected for longer, antibiotics will not speed up their recovery.\n\nThe infection can be fatal, with up to 3 per cent of newborns dying from it, according to Professor Paul Hunter, an expert in infectious diseases from the University of East Anglia.\n\nAdditionally, most babies under six months with whooping cough are hospitalised with complications, such as dehydration, breathing difficulties and pneumonia.\n\nIt is less severe in older children and adults but can still cause sore ribs, a hernias, ear infections and urinary incontinence among these groups.\n\nThe 6-in-1 vaccine, given to babies at eight, 12 and 16 weeks, and the 4-in-1 pre-school booster, administered to children aged three years and four months, is vital for protecting against catching whooping cough.\n\nPregnant women are also encouraged to get the vaccine to protect their baby from catching the infection in the first few weeks of their life.\n\nAdvertisement\n\nThe 6-in-1 vaccine, given to babies at eight, 12 and 16 weeks, and the 4-in-1 pre-school booster, administered to children aged three years and four months, is vital for protecting against catching whooping cough.\n\nPregnant women are also encouraged to get the vaccine to protect their baby from catching the infection in the first few weeks of their life.\n\nParents have been urged to check their kids have had both jabs.\n\nWithout the jabs, experts warn people risk becoming seriously ill from the infection and passing it on to others.\n\nHowever, uptake of the 6-in-1 vaccine (92.6 per cent) and 4-in-1 jab (83.3 per cent) both slumped to all-time lows in 2023, according to NHS England data.\n\nMeanwhile, just 61.5 per cent of expectant mothers had the whooping cough jab in 2022 -- the smallest number in seven years.\n\nDr Gayatri Amirthalingam, the UKHSA's consultant epidemiologist, said: 'Whooping cough can affect people of all ages but for very young babies it can be extremely serious. Our thoughts and condolences are with those families who have so tragically lost their baby.'\n\nShe added: 'Vaccination remains the best defence against whooping cough and it is vital that pregnant women and young infants receive their vaccines at the right time.\n\n'Pregnant women are offered a whooping cough vaccine in every pregnancy, ideally between 20 and 32 weeks.\n\n'This passes protection to their baby in the womb so that they are protected from birth in the first months of their life when they are most vulnerable and before they can receive their own vaccines.'\n\nMeanwhile, NHS national medical director, Professor Sir Stephen Powis, said: 'With cases of whooping cough continuing to rise sharply across the country, and today's figures sadly showing five infant deaths, it is vital that families come forward to get the protection they need.\n\n'If you are pregnant and have not been vaccinated yet, or your child is not up-to-date with whooping cough or other routine vaccinations, please contact your GP as soon as possible, and if you or your child show symptoms ask for an urgent GP appointment or get help from NHS 111.'\n\nWhooping cough is caused by the pertussis bacteria and is spread by coughing and sneezing.\n\nDoctors dish out antibiotics as treatment if the whooping cough is detected within three weeks.\n\nHowever, if a person has been infected for longer, antibiotics will not speed up their recovery.\n\nPre-pandemic, between 2,500 and 4,500 suspected cases were logged each year. This fell to around 500 during the coronavirus crisis as social distancing curbed the spread of most bugs.\n\nBut cases hit 1,728 in 2023 because of the post-pandemic rebound, in a trend that experts say is down to lower societal immunity as a result of the Covid era. Similar trends were seen for bugs like flu and RSV.\n\nHowever, rates are still nowhere near the annual high of 170,000 logged in the 1940s. Routine vaccination against whooping cough in the 1950s dramatically slashed levels.\n\nHealth officials also acknowledged cases of whooping cough rise cyclically every few years. This follows 556 cases in January and 918 in February, bringing the total number of cases in 2024 to 2,793.\n\nSadly, in the first quarter of 2024 (January - March), there have been five infant deaths. Young infants are at highest risk of severe complications and death from whooping cough. Updated estimates of vaccine effectiveness in pregnancy shows high levels of protection (92%) against infant death.\n\nDuring this quarter, while most cases (50.8%, 1420) were in those aged 15 years or older who usually get a mild illness, the rates of whooping cough remain highest in babies under 3 months of age.\n\nWhooping cough cases have been rising across England, as well as in many other countries, since December 2023 due to a combination of factors. Whooping cough is a cyclical disease that peaks every 3 to 5 years. The last cyclical increase occurred in 2016. However, in common with other diseases, cases fell to very low numbers during the pandemic due to restrictions and public behaviours. A peak year is therefore overdue. The impact of the pandemic also means there is reduced immunity in the population.\n\nUptake of vaccinations that protect against whooping cough have fallen in recent years across the country - in both the programme for pregnant women and the infant programme. Timely vaccination in pregnancy and in infancy are both important to protect vulnerable young babies from serious disease.\n\nDr Gayatri Amirthalingam, Consultant Epidemiologist at UK Health Security Agency, said:\n\nVaccination remains the best defence against whooping cough and it is vital that pregnant women and young infants receive their vaccines at the right time.\n\nPregnant women are offered a whooping cough vaccine in every pregnancy, ideally between 20 and 32 weeks. This passes protection to their baby in the womb so that they are protected from birth in the first months of their life when they are most vulnerable and before they can receive their own vaccines.\n\nAll babies are given three doses of the 6 in 1 jab at 8, 12 and 16 weeks of age to protect against whooping cough and other serious diseases such as diphtheria and polio with a pre-school booster offered at 3 years 4 months.\n\nWhooping cough can affect people of all ages but for very young babies it can be extremely serious. Our thoughts and condolences are with those families who have so tragically lost their baby.\n\nNHS National Medical Director, Professor Sir Stephen Powis, said:\n\nWith cases of whooping cough continuing to rise sharply across the country, and today's figures sadly showing five infant deaths, it is vital that families come forward to get the protection they need.\n\nIf you are pregnant and have not been vaccinated yet, or your child is not up-to-date with whooping cough or other routine vaccinations, please contact your GP as soon as possible, and if you or your child show symptoms ask for an urgent GP appointment or get help from NHS 111.\n\nWhooping cough, clinically known as pertussis, is a bacterial infection which affects the lungs. The first signs of infection are similar to a cold, such as a runny nose and sore throat, but after about a week, the infection can develop into coughing bouts that last for a few minutes and are typically worse at night. Young babies may also make a distinctive 'whoop' or have difficulty breathing after a bout of coughing, though not all babies make this noise which means whooping cough can be hard to recognise.\n\nIf anyone in your family is diagnosed with whooping cough, it's important they stay at home and do not go into work, school or nursery until 48 hours after starting antibiotics, or 3 weeks after symptoms start if they have not had antibiotics. This helps to prevent the spread of infection, especially to vulnerable groups, including infants. However, vaccination remains the best protection for babies and children.\n\n/Public Release. {"uri": "8119221582", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "13:30:39", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T13:30:39Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T13:29:17Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8156862854957581, "url": "https://www.usanews.net/breaking/what-is-whooping-cough-the-respiratory-infection-that-is-spreading-in-h154931.html", "title": "What is whooping cough, the respiratory infection that is spreading in Europe", "body": "The European Center for Disease Prevention and Control (ECDC) warned yesterday that the number of whooping cough cases in Europe has multiplied by more than ten compared to 2021 and 2022 and that \"children under six months of age are the ones \"They are at higher risk.\" According to ECDC data, Spain is the eighth EU country that has recorded the highest incidence in 2024.\n\nWhat is whooping cough? Also called pertussis or whooping cough, it is a highly contagious respiratory infection. It was very common, and a major cause of infant mortality, until the introduction of vaccines to prevent it in the mid-20th century. Unlike other common respiratory infections, which are caused by viruses, whooping cough is caused by bacteria. Specifically, from the species Bordetella pertussis.\n\nWhat symptoms does it cause? Its most characteristic symptom is a persistent cough that can last after the infection has cleared and can last between two and three months. In its initial phase, after an incubation period of between one and two weeks, the symptoms resemble those of a common cold: nasal congestion, cough, sneezing, possible low-grade fever... Infants, the most vulnerable population group, can having difficulty breathing without coughing.\n\nHow is it spread? Contagion occurs through the air through droplets that an infected person emits when coughing, sneezing or even talking. A person can be infectious from the onset of symptoms until about four weeks after the onset of cough. After this period, you can no longer be contagious because you have already eliminated the bacteria from the body, although the cough persists.\n\nHow serious is it? In adults, the infection is usually bothersome but not serious and resolves within a few weeks. In children under one year of age who are not immunized, or incompletely immunized, it can cause pneumonia, seizures and brain damage. In this age group, whooping cough is fatal in one in every 300 cases, according to data from the US Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC). In Catalonia, 22 people have been hospitalized for whooping cough in the first three months of the year, of which sixteen were under one year old, the Health Department has reported. Four have needed admission to the ICU.\n\nHow to protect young children? \"Vaccination is the best defense against whooping cough,\" declares Antoni Trilla, epidemiologist at the Hospital Cl\u00ednic in Barcelona. In Spain, the vaccination schedule provides for babies to receive three doses at 2, 4 and 11 months, and a fourth dose at six years. The pertussis immunogen is part of the DTPa vaccine, which also protects against tetanus and diphtheria. To protect newborns during the first weeks of life, pregnant women are advised to get vaccinated to transmit antibodies to the fetus through the placenta. \"It is the way to protect newborns against a serious disease against which, otherwise, they have no defense,\" says Trilla. In Spain, 88.5% of pregnant women were vaccinated in 2023, the highest rate recorded by the ECDC.\n\nDo you have treatment? Since it is an infection caused by bacteria, it is treated with antibiotics. For treatment to be effective, it should be started as soon as possible after the onset of symptoms. Antibiotics are not recommended if the cough has started more than three weeks ago since, in that case, the body has most likely already eliminated the infection. There are no specific treatments for coughing fits from whooping cough.\n\nHow do I know if the cough I have is whooping cough? \"You can have a cough for many reasons and the majority of people who have a cough do not have whooping cough,\" reassures Antoni Trilla, from the Cl\u00ednic hospital. \"But if you have a recent cough that lasts for weeks, or if there has been a case of whooping cough in our environment and we start to have a cough, then it is advisable to consult a doctor or nurse.\"\n\nHow many cases are there now in Spain? It is impossible to know because the vast majority of cases go undiagnosed and unreported. According to ECDC data, 91 cases per million inhabitants have been registered in Spain in February 2024. This figure does not report on the real incidence of infection, but it allows us to compare the current situation with that of previous periods (it is the highest figure). high in recent years) and with that of surrounding countries (it is higher than those of Portugal, France, Italy or Germany). \"It is a very high number, it means that we have many cases,\" Trilla assesses.\n\nWhy are cases increasing? The incidence of whooping cough usually increases cyclically every three to five years. The phenomenon is attributed to the fact that the population's immunity drops after several years with few infections, which creates the conditions for the bacteria to re-emerge. The figures currently recorded in Europe are close to those recorded in 2016 and 2019. Since then, measures to prevent respiratory infections during the pandemic may have favored less transmission of whooping cough and a greater decline in herd immunity .\n\nWill infections decrease soon? \"It is an endemic disease that is transmitted throughout the year, but especially in spring and early summer,\" reports Fernando Moraga-Llop, from the Spanish Association of Vaccinology. Therefore, the current epidemic wave, which began in autumn 2023, is expected to continue for a few more weeks.", "source": {"uri": "usanews.net", "dataType": "news", "title": "USANews Press Release But the rates of whooping cough remained highest in babies under three months, UKHSA noted, with 108 cases\n\nUK Health Security Agency (UKHSA) bosses received 1,319 lab-confirmed reports of cases in England in March alone. It marks a 44-fold rise on the 30 logged over the same month in 2023 and comes after MailOnline earlier this year revealed that cases of the '100 day cough' were at a decade high\n\nRiley Hughes (pictured with his mum Catherine) was otherwise healthy when he died from whooping cough aged just 32 days in 2015\n\nPregnant women are being urged to get the vaccine to protect their baby from catching the infection in the first few weeks of their life. Protection should last until they are old enough to get vaccinated themselves\n\nVaccination rates have also slumped in the wake of the pandemic in a trend experts blame on growing jab scepticism.\n\nDr Michael Head, senior research fellow in global health at the University of Southampton, today said: 'Whooping cough can and does kill babies.\n\n'A high coverage of vaccination is vital in reducing transmission within populations.\n\n'Vaccine uptake in young children and for expectant mothers during pregnancy is so important. The vaccine is safe, and extremely effective.'\n\nHe added: 'As a disease, whooping cough is as infectious as measles, and more infectious than Covid.\n\nA mother whose child died from whooping cough at just 32 days old urges action\n\nRiley Hughes was an otherwise healthy baby when he died from \u00adwhooping cough aged just 32 days in 2015.\n\nAt three weeks old, he'd \u00addeveloped a mild cold and \u00adoccasional cough. A doctor \u00adreassured his parents, Catherine and Greg, he was fine.\n\nBut Riley was sleepy and not waking for feeds, so they took him to hospital.\n\nHe was admitted and diagnosed with pertussis. His condition quickly deteriorated and he was put on life support.\n\nMs Hughes, from Perth Australia, told MailOnline: 'Riley was screaming and screaming. He would have been in a lot of pain from the needles and \u00adcannulas they were \u00adadministering.\n\n'That's how my baby will last remember the world.'\n\nShe said: 'Riley was placed in my arms The last occurred in 2016, when almost 6,000 cases were confirmed.\n\nPre-pandemic, between 2,500 and 4,500 suspected cases were logged each year. This fell to around 500 during the coronavirus crisis.\n\nLockdowns stifled the spread of pathogens as people met less.\n\nUKHSA bosses said the impact of the Covid pandemic also means there is reduced immunity in the population. This follows 556 cases in January and 918 in February, bringing the total number of cases in 2024 to 2,793.\n\nSadly, in the first quarter of 2024 (January - March), there have been five infant deaths. Young infants are at highest risk of severe complications and death from whooping cough. Updated estimates of vaccine effectiveness in pregnancy shows high levels of protection (92%) against infant death.\n\nDuring this quarter, while most cases (50.8%, 1420) were in those aged 15 years or older who usually get a mild illness, the rates of whooping cough remain highest in babies under 3 months of age.\n\nWhooping cough cases have been rising across England, as well as in many other countries, since December 2023 due to a combination of factors. Whooping cough is a cyclical disease that peaks every 3 to 5 years. The last cyclical increase occurred in 2016. However, in common with other diseases, cases fell to very low numbers during the pandemic due to restrictions and public behaviours. A peak year is therefore overdue. The impact of the pandemic also means there is reduced immunity in the population.\n\nUptake of vaccinations that protect against whooping cough have fallen in recent years across the country - in both the programme for pregnant women and the infant programme. Timely vaccination in pregnancy and in infancy are both important to protect vulnerable young babies from serious disease.\n\nDr Gayatri Amirthalingam, Consultant Epidemiologist at UK Health Security Agency, said:\n\nVaccination remains the best defence against whooping cough and it is vital that pregnant women and young infants receive their vaccines at the right time.\n\nPregnant women are offered a whooping cough vaccine in every pregnancy, ideally between 20 and 32 weeks. This passes protection to their baby in the womb so that they are protected from birth in the first months of their life when they are most vulnerable and before they can receive their own vaccines.\n\nAll babies are given three doses of the 6 in 1 jab at 8, 12 and 16 weeks of age to protect against whooping cough and other serious diseases such as diphtheria and polio with a pre-school booster offered at 3 years 4 months.\n\nWhooping cough can affect people of all ages but for very young babies it can be extremely serious. Our thoughts and condolences are with those families who have so tragically lost their baby.\n\nNHS National Medical Director, Professor Sir Stephen Powis, said:\n\nWith cases of whooping cough continuing to rise sharply across the country, and today's figures sadly showing five infant deaths, it is vital that families come forward to get the protection they need.\n\nIf you are pregnant and have not been vaccinated yet, or your child is not up-to-date with whooping cough or other routine vaccinations, please contact your GP as soon as possible, and if you or your child show symptoms ask for an urgent GP appointment or get help from NHS 111.\n\nWhooping cough, clinically known as pertussis, is a bacterial infection which affects the lungs. The first signs of infection are similar to a cold, such as a runny nose and sore throat, but after about a week, the infection can develop into coughing bouts that last for a few minutes and are typically worse at night. Young babies may also make a distinctive 'whoop' or have difficulty breathing after a bout of coughing, though not all babies make this noise which means whooping cough can be hard to recognise.\n\nIf anyone in your family is diagnosed with whooping cough, it's important they stay at home and do not go into work, school or nursery until 48 hours after starting antibiotics, or 3 weeks after symptoms start if they have not had antibiotics. This helps to prevent the spread of infection, especially to vulnerable groups, including infants. {"uri": "2024-05-346379958", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-06", "time": "11:06:01", "dateTime": "2024-05-06T11:06:01Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-06T11:00:19Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8078431487083435, "url": "https://eu.registerguard.com/story/news/local/2024/05/06/whooping-cough-oregon/73471866007/", "title": "What is whooping cough? Spike in cases prompts warning from Lane County health experts", "body": "Whooping cough is the common name for pertussis, a highly contagious respiratory disease caused by a bacteria. It is usually treated with antibiotics.\n\nLane County health officials are warning residents about a recent uptick in pertussis, also known as whooping cough, after 13 cases were reported in April.\n\nAccording to Lane County Public Health, recent cases indicate community spread of the highly contagious respiratory infection.\n\nHealth officials recommend people stay up to date on pertussis vaccinations.\n\n\"This is very important for infants, young children, those with underlying medical conditions and their close contacts,\" Lane County said in statement. \"We want to protect these vulnerable groups who are more likely to develop severe whooping cough.\"\n\nWhat is whooping cough?\n\nWhooping cough is a respiratory infection caused by bacteria that can cause severe coughing fits followed by a high-pitched \"whoop\" sound when breathing in. The cough can last for up to 10 weeks or more.\n\nSome infants who have pertussis may not cough at all but instead have apnea or struggles with breathing.\n\nVomiting and exhaustion also are symptoms of pertussis, which can lead to serious complications, especially for infants, according to Lane County Public Health. Other complications include pneumonia, dehydration, seizures and brain damage.\n\nOregon sees a decline in vaccination rates\n\nLane County Health officials said vaccination rates have been declining statewide since the pandemic, which increases the risk for continued spread of diseases in the community.\n\nThe pertussis vaccines are known as DTaP, which stands for diphtheria, tetanus and pertussis; as well as Tdap, which is a similar vaccine but for adolescents and adults.\n\nAccording to data from the Oregon Immunization Program, 93.9 percent of kindergarten students in Lane County were vaccinated for DTaP in 2022, while 91.5 percent of kindergarten students received the vaccine in 2023.\n\nThere also was a decline in complete vaccination rates for kindergarten-aged students in Lane County from 92.1 percent in 2022 to 88.3 percent in 2023.\n\nComplete vaccinations include DTaP, Hepatitis A, Hepatitis B, MMR (1st dose of Measles, Mumps, and Rubella), 2nd dose of Measles, Polio and Varicella.\n\nDTaP and Tdap vaccination rates in Lane County for K-12 students during the 2022-2023 academic year were on par with the Oregon state average of 93.9 percent.\n\nComplete immunization rates in Lane County were higher than the state average at 92.3 percent, compared to Oregon's 91.8 percent. Of the K-12 student population in Lane County, 6.6 percent of students had a nonmedical exemption from any school-required vaccine.\n\nPertussis cases rise across Oregon\n\nAs of April 14, the Oregon Health Authority reported a significant rise in Pertussis cases. Year-to-date case counts in Oregon, from January to March of 2024, shows at least 60 cases of pertussis statewide. In comparison, 2022 saw at least 17 cases of pertussis within the same time frame.\n\nPertussis cases reported so far in 2024 are the highest recorded since 2020, where at least 145 cases were recorded from January to March of that year.\n\nHow to get vaccinated for whooping cough\n\nAccording to Lane County health officials, pertussis vaccinations can usually be obtained from a primary care provider such as a pediatrician, family physician, internal medicine physician or nurse practitioner.\n\nResidents who do not have a primary care provider can contact a pharmacy or their health plan to review vaccination options, while people without insurance can contact a federally qualified health center to review their vaccine options.\n\nIf your child is experiencing symptoms of pertussis such as runny nose, fever and violent coughing, residents are advised to see a doctor as soon as possible, said Lane County Public Health. If your child is struggling to breathe or is turning blue or purple, call 9-1-1 immediately.\n\nLane County recommends infants and children complete a series of DTaP doses, while adolescents receive one dose of Tdap. Women who are in their third trimester of pregnancy also are referred to the Tdap dose to help protect their babies early in life.\n\nParents who are hesitant about the pertussis vaccine are encouraged to talk with their child's health care providers to discuss their concerns.\n\nBacteria spread and prevention\n\nIf you are diagnosed with pertussis, Lane County officials recommend you take antibiotics as prescribed and avoid contact with others until you are no longer contagious, which can last for up to three weeks after coughing fits begin.\n\nLane County Public Health recommends {"uri": "2024-05-348203012", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-07", "time": "20:30:31", "dateTime": "2024-05-07T20:30:31Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-07T17:25:53Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8078431487083435, "url": "https://www.ireland-live.ie/news/uk/1495478/whooping-cough-cases-could-reach-40-year-high-in-2024-experts-warn.html", "title": "Whooping cough cases 'could reach 40-year high' in 2024, experts warn", "body": "Cases of whooping cough could reach a 40-year high in 2024, experts have warned amid a rapid rise in cases.\n\nThe number of cases of whooping cough in England was higher in February than in the whole of 2023.\n\nThe bacterial infection, also known as pertussis, affects the lungs and breathing tubes.\n\nWhooping cough can be called the \"100-day cough\" because of how long it can take to recover from it.\n\nIt spreads very easily and can sometimes cause serious problems.\n\nThere were 913 laboratory-confirmed cases in England in February, up from 555 in January.\n\nThis is compared with the 858 cases for the whole of 2023.\n\nFigures for March will be released by the UK Health Security Agency on Thursday.\n\nPaul Hunter, professor in medicine at the University of East Anglia (UEA), said: \"For most adults the whooping cough is not life threatening, though can be very unpleasant.\n\n\"This is a chronic repeated bout of coughing which can be so bad that people feel it a struggle to breathe in again.\n\n\"It used to be much more common in the last century up until the vaccine was introduced.\n\n\"However, this current year looks like we may see more cases than we have seen in any of the last 40 years.\"\n\nProf Hunter said that a number of factors could be behind the rise in cases, including: a drop in vaccine uptake; reduced \"population immunity\" due to a fall in cases linked to social distancing measures during the pandemic; and a \"scare over vaccines\" in the early 2000s which led to a group of people aged around 21 who did not complete their vaccination.\n\nHe added: \"The infection can affect anyone who is not vaccinated and even some that are.\n\n\"However, the main risk of death or severe long-term complications is seen in young children, especially those under three months old.\n\n\"It is this age group that are most at risk of death and developing longer-term problems such as brain damage.\n\n\"The problem is that this age group is too young for the vaccine in most circumstances.\n\n\"That is why we offer vaccine to pregnant women. Not to protect them but to protect their babies during the riskiest first months of life.\n\n\"Vaccine uptake in pregnant women has been falling quite markedly in recent years.\"\n\nOf the 1,468 cases confirmed between January and February 2024, around half were in those aged 15 years or older and 30% were in children aged between 10 and 14 years.\n\nDuring the first two months of the year there were 52 confirmed cases among babies under the age of three months - who are at highest risk of serious complications.\n\nDr Michael Head, senior research fellow in global health at the University of Southampton, said: \"A drop in vaccination coverage will be a key factor contributing to this rise in cases of whooping cough.\n\n\"It's vital that vulnerable groups such as babies and pregnant women are up to date with their recommended immunisations, and that includes the pertussis vaccine.\n\n\"Whooping cough can be a very severe infection, potentially fatal to babies who become {"uri": "8118947334", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-09", "time": "10:30:43", "dateTime": "2024-05-09T10:30:43Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-09T10:28:30Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7960784435272217, "url": "https://www.yahoo.com/news/whooping-cough-symptoms-deadly-102420362.html", "title": "What is whooping cough, what are the symptoms and how deadly is it?", "body": "The name whooping cough might conjure up images of Victorian England, but the disease is currently having a resurgence - with five baby deaths.\n\nMore than 2,700 whooping cough cases have been reported across England so far in 2024 - more than three times the amount recorded in the whole of last year.\n\nUK Health Security Agency (UKHSA) figures show there were 2,793 cases reported to the end of March. This is compared with the 858 cases for the whole of 2023.\n\nBetween July and the end of November 2023, there were 1,141 suspected cases of whooping cough in England and Wales, as reported by clinicians, nearly 700 more than the equivalent timeframes in 2021 and 2022.\n\nThe disease has spread across Europe. The European Centre for Disease Prevention and Control said that the bloc had seen more cases in the first three months of 2024 than an average year between 2012 and 2019.\n\nIt confirmed on Wednesday there had been 11 infant deaths and eight deaths among older adults on the continent.\n\nWhooping cough is a cyclical disease that peaks every 3-5 years. The last cyclical increase occurred in 2016, with a major outbreak last seen in 2012.\n\nHealth officials said that the current deadly outbreak is \"overdue\" following suppression of the spread of disease during the pandemic due to lockdown and other restrictions.\n\nThe UK Health Security Agency (UKHSA) said \"the impact of the pandemic also means there is reduced immunity in the population\".\n\nThe name is given to a bacterial chest infection which can last for up to 100 days. \"It's highly contagious and it's spread via respiratory droplets,\" says Dr Safia Debar, executive health GP at Mayo Clinic Healthcare in London.\n\nThe illness progresses through three stages, the first being largely similar to a common cold with symptoms such as a runny nose, sore eyes and sneezing. After two weeks, infected individuals begin to develop bouts of intense coughing which can last for several minutes, sometimes followed by an audible whoop or gasp for breath between coughs. Young children may turn blue or grey due to difficulty breathing while adults might become red in the face.\n\nSufferers can then be left with a chronic cough which remains for several weeks.\n\nDebar recommends that anyone with a persistent cough which has lasted for more than two weeks, should see a doctor.\n\n\"Particularly if you also have symptoms like lethargy and fever, or you've been in contact with somebody with whooping cough, then definitely seek medical attention,\" she says. \"Without treatment, people are infectious for three weeks, but with antibiotics, you're not infectious after five days.\"\n\nThe illness is most dangerous to newborn babies and infants. Most children under six months require hospitalisation after contracting whooping cough due to the risks of brain swelling and convulsions, and 1-3 per cent of those under three months die from the illness.\n\n\"Babies are vulnerable because their immune systems haven't fully developed,\" explains Dr Dipender Gill, a medical doctor and researcher at Imperial College London. \"It's quite prevalent in school-aged children as well, so those who are 11 to 18 years old.\"\n\nIn teenagers and adults, serious cases of whooping cough can lead to pneumonia, fainting and even rib fractures due to the intensity of the coughing fits. However, Gill says it tends to be the elderly and those with compromised immune systems who are most vulnerable.\n\n\"The whole population is susceptible to getting the infection,\" says Gill. \"But I think it can lead to particularly severe symptoms in elderly people, because as you age, your immune system becomes more impaired or if your immune system was impaired for other reasons such as cancer treatment or transplantation and immunosuppressive therapy.\"\n\nBecause whooping cough is so infectious, Debar recommends self-isolating if your doctor suspects that you have the illness, at least for five days while antibiotic treatment kicks in.\n\nHowever, the most effective preventable measure is to get vaccinated. Babies at eight, 12 and 16 weeks old are eligible for an immunisation to whooping cough on the NHS along with other vaccines. An additional booster shot can be given when the child is three.\n\nExperts are particularly keen to raise awareness that the whooping cough vaccine is now recommended for pregnant women.\n\n\"Newborns and babies in the first few months of life are more likely to get severe disease, [and] this is almost entirely preventable if the mother is vaccinated in pregnancy and her protective antibodies can then reach the baby through the placenta, and protect [it] until the baby gets its own vaccines,\" says Beate Kampmann, professor of paediatric infection & immunity at the London School of Hygiene and Tropical Medicine.\n\nKampmann recommends checking your vaccine records in case you missed receiving this jab. It is also possible for concerned older individuals to receive a booster vaccine for whooping cough.\n\nDebar says that doctors will typically do a throat swab to see whether someone is infected with the Bordetella pertussis bacterium and if so, prescribe antibiotics.\n\n\"Antibiotics are given on a case-by-case basis,\" she says. \"After the first three weeks, the infection tends to wane and sometimes if you're just left with the chronic cough, it could be that you do not need antibiotics.\"\n\nShe says that once any fever has passed, it's not necessary to remain in bed the whole time, and instead there are certain remedies which can be taken to try and alleviate the coughing.\n\n\"You want to strengthen your immune system,\" says Debar. \"Some people will use cough remedies, we find that they don't really work. But things like saline sprays can sometimes provide symptomatic relief. I love natural remedies, so ginger, turmeric, all things to strengthen immunity.\n\n\"The coughing can be worse at night so you want the environment around you to be as moist as possible. So, use a humidifier, make sure the temperature is appropriate, and make sure you're well hydrated. Maybe try an extra pillow if that helps.\"\n\nThe last major outbreak in England was in 2012 when 14 deaths were reported in one year.\n\nSince then, vaccinations have been introduced, with all the deaths occurring in those too young for vaccination.\n\nThe whooping cough vaccine is routinely given as part of the: 6-in-1 vaccine - for babies at 8, 12 and 16 weeks.\n\nIn 2012, vaccination of pregnant women was introduced, to protect the youngest babies. However, vaccination rates have been falling in recent years.\n\nExperts are concerned that the current outbreak is being fuelled by a combination of factors: vaccine hesitancy, suppression of disease linked to social distancing and reduced \"population immunity\" as a result.\n\nProfessor Paul Hunter, professor of medicine at the University of East Anglia (UEA), said the disease was much more common in the last century until vaccines were introduced\n\n\"This current year looks like we may see more cases than we have seen in any of the last 40 years,\" he said.\n\nProf Hunter said: \"The infection can affect anyone who is not vaccinated and even some that are. However, the main risk of death or severe long-term complications is seen in young children, especially those under three months old.\n\n\"It is this age group that is most at risk of death and developing longer-term problems such as brain damage.\n\n\"The problem is that this age group is too young for the vaccine in most circumstances.\n\n\"That is why we offer vaccines to pregnant women. Not to protect them but to protect their babies during the riskiest first months of life.\n\n\"Vaccine uptake in pregnant women has been falling quite markedly in recent years.\"", "source": {"uri": It used to be called the \"100-day cough\" as symptoms can last for months if left untreated.\n\nWhat are symptoms?\n\nWhooping cough causes intense coughing fits that can even lead to choking or vomiting.
